Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Maturidi View on Partnership Between God and Servant
The author presents the Mu'tazilite objection and then refutes it using the property analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi doctrine of human action does not necessitate associating partners with God, and how the property analogy clarifies this.

Core Idea
Partnership requires independence; God's enabling of the servant does not create a partner.

The Mu'tazilites accused the Maturidis of making humans partners with God in creation. In this passage, the author clarifies that the Maturidi position does not entail partnership at all. He uses a powerful analogy from property to show that the same thing can be under two different ownerships without partnership.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection
The passage begins by presenting the Mu'tazilite claim hypothetically: if an action were invented solely by the human with no divine power, then the combination of two powers would make humans partners with God.
This is the view the Maturidis reject.
2
Examine the Maturidi response
The author then clarifies his own view: the same action is within both God's power (by creation) and the servant's power (by God's enabling). This does not necessitate partnership.
3
Understand the property analogy
The author compares this to property: what a servant owns is also owned by God, yet rational people do not consider that partnership. This shows that shared power does not imply partnership.
The analogy is central to the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفعل يخترعه العبد لا قدرة الله تعالى عليه وحصول الفعل بمجموع مقدوريهما، فكان الأحياء شركاء الله تعالى في تخليق العالم، وكل حي من كل فعل يفعله شريك الله تعالى. فأما ما قلنا فلا يوجب الشركة إذ عين ما هو مقدور الله تعالى مقدور العبد بإقداره، وما هو مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى، وهذا لا يعقل شركة كما في الملك إذ هو ما ملك العبد، عينه ملك الله وما هو ملك الله تعالى من الأعيان التي جعلها ملكًا لعباده عينه ملك العبد ولم يعد ذلك شركة في الملك بإجماع العقلاء. المعتزلة يثبتون شركاء الله بقولهم وبالوقوف على حقيقة الشركة علم أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا الله تعالى في العالم شركاء ومن كل فعل اختياري شريكا، ونحن بحمد الله تعالى برآء عن ذلك، فكانت نسبة المعتزلة إيانا إلى القول مما يوجب الشركة ووصف أنفسهم بالبراءة من ذلك وقاحة عظيمة أو جهلاً بحقيقة الشركة المعقولة، وصاروا بإثبات الشركاء الله تعالى في العالم وفي كل فعل مساعدين للمجوس بل كانوا شرا من المجوس من وجهين أحدهما أن المجوس ما أثبتوا الله إلا شريكاً واحداً، وهؤلاء جعلوا كل ما دب ودرج من ذوي الأرواح والمهج شركاء الله تعالى. والثاني: أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله تعالى عما هو عندهم سفه، وما أضافوا إلى الشريك إلا الشرور والقبائح، والمعتزلة أضافوا كل ما هو حسن على الحقيقة إلى غير الله تعالى، وجعلوا ما فعله غيره أحسن مما فعله الله تعالى على ما قررنا والله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة. وفي المسألة دلائل كثيرة ذكرها من تقدم من ائمتنا الماضين - قدس الله أرواحهم – ومن ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من أرباب المذاهب وغيرهم لا وجه إلى ذكر عشرها فضلاً عن كلها لما يقتضي حصرها وذكرها بما للخصوم عليها من الشبه ودفعها، والكشف عن بطلانها كتابًا مفردًا يربو على هذا الكتاب الذي نحن بصدده، ولو رحمه الله كشفنا عما للإمام أبي منصور الماتريدي من الإشارات اللفظية والعبارات الوجيزة الجارية مجرى التوفيقات في هذا الباب لطال الكلام وملت عن ضبطها الأفهام.
And an action that the servant invents, with no power of God over it, and the occurrence of the action by the combination of their two powers, then living beings would be partners of God in the creation of the world, and every living being from every action it does is a partner of God. As for what we say, it does not necessitate partnership, since the very thing that is within God's power is within the servant's power by His enabling, and what is within the servant's power is within God's power, and this is not understood as partnership just as in property: what the servant owns is the same thing that God owns, and what is owned by God from the entities that He made property for His servants is the same thing the servant owns, and that is not counted as partnership in property by consensus of the rational. The Mu'tazilites affirm partners for God by their statement, and by understanding the reality of partnership it is known that the Mu'tazilites are the ones who affirmed partners for God in the world and a partner from every voluntary action, and we, praise be to God, are innocent of that. So the Mu'tazilites' attribution to us of a statement that necessitates partnership and their description of themselves as innocent of that is great impudence or ignorance of the understood reality of partnership. And they, by affirming partners for God in the world and in every action, became helpers of the Magians, rather they were worse than the Magians in two respects: first, that the Magians only affirmed one partner for God, whereas these made every creeping and moving thing with souls and spirits a partner of God. Second, that the Magians intended by affirming the partner to exonerate God from what they considered folly, and they only attributed evil and abominable things to the partner, whereas the Mu'tazilites attributed every really good thing to other than God, and made what another did better than what God did, as we have established, and God is the protection and salvation from every error and innovation. And there are many proofs in this issue mentioned by those among our past imams - may God sanctify their souls - and those who helped us in this issue from the leaders of schools and others. There is no way to mention even a tenth of them, let alone all of them, because their enumeration and mention, along with the specious arguments of the opponents against them and their refutation, and the exposure of their falsity, would require a separate book surpassing this book we are engaged in. And if he had mercy on us, we would have disclosed what Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi indicated by verbal allusions and concise phrases running in the course of reconciliations in this chapter, but the speech would have become lengthy and the minds would have deviated from grasping them.
Source pages p. 940

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite charge that the Maturidi doctrine of human action entails associating partners with God. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazilite objection in their own terms: if a human action were invented solely by the human with no power of God over it, and the action occurs by the combination of two powers, then every living being would be a partner of God in creation. Then the author clarifies that their own position does not necessitate partnership. The key point is that what is in God's power is also in the servant's power by God's enabling (إقدار), and what is in the servant's power is also in God's power. This is not partnership, just as property: the same thing can be owned by both God and the servant without partnership being affirmed by rational consensus. Then the author turns the accusation around: it is the Mu'tazilites who actually affirm partners for God, because they posit an action outside God's power. We are innocent of that. Their calling us partners is either impudence or ignorance. The Mu'tazilites are worse than the Magians (Zoroastrians) in two ways: (1) the Magians only affirmed one partner for God, while the Mu'tazilites make every living being a partner; (2) the Magians attributed only evil to their partner, while the Mu'tazilites attribute real goodness to other than God, implying that what others do is better than what God does. The author concludes by noting that many proofs exist from earlier imams, but to mention them and refute counterarguments would require a separate book, and even the concise indications of al-Maturidi on this topic would be too lengthy to explain fully.

Key Terms
شركة
مقدور
إقدار
مقدور العبد
مقدور الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view makes humans independent partners with God in creation.
Correction: The author shows that shared power does not imply partnership because the servant's power depends on God's enabling, just as shared ownership does not imply partnership when one party's ownership derives from the other.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the Maturidi view not entail partnership?
A) Because humans have no real power.
B) Because the servant's power is independent of God's.
C) Because what is in God's power is also in the servant's power by God's enabling, similar to property ownership.
D) Because God has no power over human actions.

Key Takeaway
Shared power over an action does not equate to partnership because the servant's power depends entirely on God's enabling, just as a servant's property belongs ultimately to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something significant—whether in work, worship, or daily life—do you genuinely feel that it was God who enabled you, or do you silently credit your own ability? If the latter, what would it take for you to see your success as entirely from God without slipping into a sense of partnership? Commit today to saying 'Alhamdulillah' with full presence of heart, acknowledging that your power to act is only by His enablement.
?
Think of a moment you pridefully thought 'I did that' or 'I earned that.' Does that pride reveal an unspoken belief that your actions are independent from God? How does this lesson's claim—that even your owned property is still ultimately God's—challenge your sense of ownership and self-reliance? What one step can you take tomorrow to practice seeing your actions and possessions as trusts from God alone?
?
The passage uses the property analogy to argue that shared ownership does not imply partnership. But do you treat your time, talent, and wealth as if you have an independent claim over them? If God truly owns everything and only lends it to you, how should that reshape your willingness to spend it in His way? Write down one concrete way you will share what you 'own' today in a manner that reflects His sole Lordship.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Mu'tazilites Are the True Associators
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite charge and argues that the Mu'tazilites themselves are guilty of shirk, exceeding even the Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the accusation back on the Mu'tazilites and compares them unfavorably to the Magians.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites, not the Maturidis, actually affirm partners for God, and they are worse than the Magians.

Having defended the Maturidi position, the author now goes on the offensive. He argues that it is the Mu'tazilites who truly posit partners for God by denying His power over human actions. Moreover, they are worse than the Magians (Zoroastrians) in both the number of partners and what they attribute to them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counter-accusation
The author states that by their own logic, the Mu'tazilites affirm partners for God, because they posit actions that are not under God's power.
This is a reversal of the original charge.
2
Compare with the Magians
The author compares the Mu'tazilites to the Magians (Zoroastrians) and says they are worse in two specific ways.
Magians believed in two gods, one evil and one good.
3
Reflect on the two ways
First, Magians only had one partner, while Mu'tazilites make every living being a partner. Second, Magians attributed only evil to their partner, but Mu'tazilites attribute genuine goodness to other than God.
This shows the severity of the Mu'tazilite error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفعل يخترعه العبد لا قدرة الله تعالى عليه وحصول الفعل بمجموع مقدوريهما، فكان الأحياء شركاء الله تعالى في تخليق العالم، وكل حي من كل فعل يفعله شريك الله تعالى. فأما ما قلنا فلا يوجب الشركة إذ عين ما هو مقدور الله تعالى مقدور العبد بإقداره، وما هو مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى، وهذا لا يعقل شركة كما في الملك إذ هو ما ملك العبد، عينه ملك الله وما هو ملك الله تعالى من الأعيان التي جعلها ملكًا لعباده عينه ملك العبد ولم يعد ذلك شركة في الملك بإجماع العقلاء. المعتزلة يثبتون شركاء الله بقولهم وبالوقوف على حقيقة الشركة علم أن المعتزلة هم الذين أثبتوا الله تعالى في العالم شركاء ومن كل فعل اختياري شريكا، ونحن بحمد الله تعالى برآء عن ذلك، فكانت نسبة المعتزلة إيانا إلى القول مما يوجب الشركة ووصف أنفسهم بالبراءة من ذلك وقاحة عظيمة أو جهلاً بحقيقة الشركة المعقولة، وصاروا بإثبات الشركاء الله تعالى في العالم وفي كل فعل مساعدين للمجوس بل كانوا شرا من المجوس من وجهين أحدهما أن المجوس ما أثبتوا الله إلا شريكاً واحداً، وهؤلاء جعلوا كل ما دب ودرج من ذوي الأرواح والمهج شركاء الله تعالى. والثاني: أن المجوس أرادوا بإثبات الشريك تنزيه الله تعالى عما هو عندهم سفه، وما أضافوا إلى الشريك إلا الشرور والقبائح، والمعتزلة أضافوا كل ما هو حسن على الحقيقة إلى غير الله تعالى، وجعلوا ما فعله غيره أحسن مما فعله الله تعالى على ما قررنا والله العصمة والنجاة عن كل ضلالة وبدعة. وفي المسألة دلائل كثيرة ذكرها من تقدم من ائمتنا الماضين - قدس الله أرواحهم – ومن ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من أرباب المذاهب وغيرهم لا وجه إلى ذكر عشرها فضلاً عن كلها لما يقتضي حصرها وذكرها بما للخصوم عليها من الشبه ودفعها، والكشف عن بطلانها كتابًا مفردًا يربو على هذا الكتاب الذي نحن بصدده، ولو رحمه الله كشفنا عما للإمام أبي منصور الماتريدي من الإشارات اللفظية والعبارات الوجيزة الجارية مجرى التوفيقات في هذا الباب لطال الكلام وملت عن ضبطها الأفهام.
And an action that the servant invents, with no power of God over it, and the occurrence of the action by the combination of their two powers, then living beings would be partners of God in the creation of the world, and every living being from every action it does is a partner of God. As for what we say, it does not necessitate partnership, since the very thing that is within God's power is within the servant's power by His enabling, and what is within the servant's power is within God's power, and this is not understood as partnership just as in property: what the servant owns is the same thing that God owns, and what is owned by God from the entities that He made property for His servants is the same thing the servant owns, and that is not counted as partnership in property by consensus of the rational. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite charge that the Maturidi doctrine of human action entails associating partners with God. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazilite objection in their own terms: if a human action were invented solely by the human with no power of God over it, and the action occurs by the combination of two powers, then every living being would be a partner of God in creation. Then the author clarifies that their own position does not necessitate partnership. The key point is that what is in God's power is also in the servant's power by God's enabling (إقدار), and what is in the servant's power is also in God's power. This is not partnership, just as property: the same thing can be owned by both God and the servant without partnership being affirmed by rational consensus. Then the author turns the accusation around: it is the Mu'tazilites who actually affirm partners for God, because they posit an action outside God's power. We are innocent of that. Their calling us partners is either impudence or ignorance. The Mu'tazilites are worse than the Magians (Zoroastrians) in two ways: (1) the Magians only affirmed one partner for God, while the Mu'tazilites make every living being a partner; (2) the Magians attributed only evil to their partner, while the Mu'tazilites attribute real goodness to other than God, implying that what others do is better than what God does. The author concludes by noting that many proofs exist from earlier imams, but to mention them and refute counterarguments would require a separate book, and even the concise indications of al-Maturidi on this topic would be too lengthy to explain fully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view leads to shirk.
Correction: The author shows it is the Mu'tazilite view that actually leads to shirk by making humans independent partners with God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in what two ways are the Mu'tazilites worse than the Magians?
A) The Magians believed in many gods, while the Mu'tazilites believe in one.
B) The Magians only affirmed one partner, while the Mu'tazilites make every living being a partner, and the Magians attributed only evil to their partner, while the Mu'tazilites attribute good to other than God.
C) The Mu'tazilites are more rational than the Magians.
D) The Magians denied God's attributes, while the Mu'tazilites affirmed them.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' own doctrine of human action leads to affirming multiple partners for God, making them worse than the Magians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says attributing good actions to yourself rather than God is a form of shirk. When you accomplish something praiseworthy, do you really believe God created that action, or do you secretly feel you did it on your own? How can you train your heart to see every good deed as God's creation alone?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's justice by denying His control over evil actions. Have you ever tried to 'protect' God from something by limiting His power in your mind? Repent and affirm that God is the Creator of all actions, good and bad, without compromising His justice.
?
If you think any of your achievements are independent of God's power, you have partnered Him with yourself. What specific accomplishment are you most proud of? In prayer today, verbally acknowledge that God alone created that success and ask Him to keep you from pride.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Denial of Divine Creation of Actions
The author enumerates three possible grounds for the Mu'tazilite denial and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible grounds for the Mu'tazilite denial that God creates human actions and how the author refutes each.

Core Idea
God creates our voluntary actions—this does not negate our free choice.

The Mu'tazilites denied that God creates human actions, but on what basis? The author identifies only three possible reasons for their denial: lack of evidence, impossibility, or undesirable consequences. He then systematically refutes each, showing that their position is untenable. This passage clarifies a crucial point in the debate on divine predestination and human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's argument structure.
The Arabic text ends abruptly; the argument is completed in the commentary.
2
Identify the three grounds
The author states that the Mu'tazilite denial can only be based on: (1) lack of evidence, (2) impossibility, or (3) that it entails compulsion and nullifies obligation. Find each in the text.
3
Examine the refutations
Note how the author refutes each ground: for lack of evidence, he claims proofs have been given; for impossibility, he demands evidence and says their arguments have been answered; for compulsion, he argues that divine creation does not negate free choice because God creates voluntary actions, not necessary ones.
The author also points out that the Mu'tazilites' position would deny necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترديد يثبت القول بعجز المعتزلة ― ومحصول المسألة: أن إنكار تخليقها من الله تعالى، إما أن يكون لما لا دلالة عليها أو للإحالة أو لما في القول به من إيجاب الضرورة وامتناع التكليف وارتفاع الأمر والنهي. فإن كان إنكارهم لعدم الدلالة فقد أقمنا الدلائل السمعية والعقلية ما فيه كفاية لمن نصح نفسه ولم يكابر عقله، وإن كان إنكارهم للإحالة فيطالبون بدليلها، ولا دليل معهم سوى أنه لا يتصور في أوهامهم لكون الشيء عندهم فعلاً لفاعلين وتعلق قدرتين به أو لأن القول به يوجب الشركة، وقد تقصينا عن عهدة كل ذلك وإن كان إنكارهم لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة فقد أجبنا عن ذلك ودفعنا الشبه بحمد الله، على أن العلم بكوننا مختارين ثابت بطريق الضرورة وثبوت الاختيار لا ينفي التخليق لما مر على أن التخليق كيف ينفي الاختيار وهو تعالى يخلق الفعل الاختياري لا الضروري وبينهما مفارقة، والقول بأنه تعالى يريد تخليق أحدهما فيحصل الآخر شاء أو أبى إثبات الاضطرار والعجز للصانع جل وعلا، فكان في جعل الفعل الاختياري ضروريا لحصوله بالتخليق إنكارهم علم الضروريات وجعل
Repetition affirming the statement about the incapacity of the Mu'tazilites – The summary of the issue: that the denial of the creation of actions from God is either due to there being no evidence for it, or due to impossibility, or due to what the statement entails of necessitating compulsion and the impossibility of obligation and the removal of command and prohibition. If their denial is for lack of evidence, then we have presented scriptural and rational proofs that suffice for one who advises himself and does not contradict his intellect. If their denial is for impossibility, then they are required to produce evidence for it, and they have no evidence except that it is inconceivable in their minds because the thing in their view is an act of two agents and the attachment of two powers to it, or because the statement entails partnership. And we have fully discharged that. And if their denial is because of what it entails of necessitating compulsion, then we have answered that and refuted the specious arguments, praise be to God. For the knowledge that we are volitional agents is established by way of necessity, and the establishment of volition does not negate divine creation, as has passed, because how can divine creation negate volition when He creates the voluntary action, not the necessary one, and there is a distinction between them. And the statement that He wills to create one of them, then the other occurs whether He wills or not, is an affirmation of compulsion and incapacity for the Craftsman, exalted and high. So by making the voluntary action necessary due to its occurrence through divine creation, their denial is a denial of necessary knowledge and making...
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage is a summary and refutation of the Mu'tazilite denial that God creates human actions. The author states that such denial can only be based on one of three grounds: (1) lack of evidence, (2) impossibility (استحالة), or (3) undesirable consequences such as compulsion (اضطرار) and nullification of religious obligations, commands, and prohibitions. The author then refutes each. Against lack of evidence: we have provided both scriptural and rational proofs sufficient for those who are sincere. Against impossibility: the Mu'tazilites' only argument is that it is inconceivable (in their minds) that one action could be from two agents or have two powers attached, or that it entails partnership; the author claims these have been fully addressed earlier. Against the consequence of compulsion: the author argues that the knowledge that we have free choice (اختيار) is itself necessary (ضروري), and this does not negate divine creation (تخليق) because God creates the voluntary action, not a necessary one. There is a difference between voluntary and necessary actions. If the Mu'tazilites insist that God's creating the action would make it necessary, then they are the ones who are making the voluntary action necessary, which negates necessary knowledge. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazilites' own position leads to denying evident truths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates my actions, then I have no free choice.
Correction: The author argues that God creates the voluntary action, not the necessary one; thus, human choice remains intact. Divine creation does not negate volition.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the third possible ground for the Mu'tazilites' denial of divine creation of actions?
A) Lack of evidence
B) Impossibility
C) That it necessitates compulsion and negates obligation and command
D) That it leads to associating partners with God

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite denial of divine creation of actions is based on only three possible grounds, all of which the author refutes, establishing that divine creation does not negate human free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that God created that action while you were freely choosing it? How does that change the way you view your responsibility? What will you do today to own your choices more consciously before God?
?
The Mu'tazilites were afraid that if God creates actions, He would be the author of evil. In your own life, do you ever subtly blame God for your mistakes or bad habits? How can you train yourself to attribute your faults to your own free will instead?
?
The author says knowledge of our free choice is 'by necessity'. Think of a moment today when you made a decision—why did you feel that you could have chosen otherwise? Does that feeling of choice coexist with your belief in God's creation of all things? How can you live in a way that honors both His sovereignty and your accountability?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Three Reasons for Denying Divine Creation of Actions
Al-Nasafi enumerates three possible reasons for the Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of actions and refutes the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities why the Mu'tazila might deny that God creates human actions, and al-Nasafi's refutation of the first two.

Core Idea
Denial can only stem from ignorance, impossibility, or compulsion — but all three collapse.

Al-Nasafi categorizes the possible reasons for the Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of actions. He shows that each basis is unfounded, setting the stage for a deeper response to the most common objection: that creation implies compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reasons
Read the passage carefully and note the three possible reasons al-Nasafi lists: lack of evidence, impossibility, and necessitation of compulsion.
These are introduced by the phrases 'لعدم الدلالة', 'للإحالة', and 'لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة'.
2
Examine the response to the first reason
See how al-Nasafi responds to the claim of lack of evidence: he states that sufficient scriptural and rational proofs have already been provided.
He does not repeat the proofs here, just asserts their existence.
3
Examine the response to the second reason
For the impossibility claim, al-Nasafi demands evidence and notes that the Mu'tazila have no proof other than subjective inconceivability or the charge of shirk, which he has already refuted.
He ends this part by saying he has discharged responsibility for all that.
4
Prepare for the third reason
The third reason—compulsion—is the most serious. Al-Nasafi will address it in the next lesson, but note that he introduces it here as the final category.
This lesson covers only the first two responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحصول المسألة: أن إنكار تخليقها من الله تعالى، إما أن يكون لما لا دلالة عليها أو للإحالة أو لما في القول به من إيجاب الضرورة وامتناع التكليف وارتفاع الأمر والنهي. فإن كان إنكارهم لعدم الدلالة فقد أقمنا الدلائل السمعية والعقلية ما فيه كفاية لمن نصح نفسه ولم يكابر عقله، وإن كان إنكارهم للإحالة فيطالبون بدليلها، ولا دليل معهم سوى أنه لا يتصور في أوهامهم لكون الشيء عندهم فعلاً لفاعلين وتعلق قدرتين به أو لأن القول به يوجب الشركة، وقد تقصينا عن عهدة كل ذلك وإن كان إنكارهم لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة فقد أجبنا عن ذلك ودفعنا الشبه بحمد الله، على أن العلم بكوننا مختارين ثابت بطريق الضرورة وثبوت الاختيار لا ينفي التخليق لما مر على أن التخليق كيف ينفي الاختيار وهو تعالى يخلق الفعل الاختياري لا الضروري وبينهما مفارقة، والقول بأنه تعالى يريد تخليق أحدهما فيحصل الآخر شاء أو أبى إثبات الاضطرار والعجز للصانع جل وعلا، فكان في جعل الفعل الاختياري ضروريا لحصوله بالتخليق إنكارهم علم الضروريات وجعل الباري مضطرًا في تخليقه، فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ قلنا : العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن معلوم الباري، فكيف يكون مختارا والباري لا يخرج أيضا عن معلومه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ وجاء من هذا أنه تعالى لما لم يكن في فعله مضطرًا وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأنه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه فمعلومه أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلو صار بعلمه مضطرًا لانقلب جاهلاً حيث علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره، فلم يفعل بل فعل مضطرًا فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأن معلومه تعالى أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره غير مضطر ، فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً وذلك محال، فبقى العبد مختارا فكذا لما خلق بعلمه الاختياري يبقى مختاراً ؛ إذ لو انقلب بذلك مضطرًا لم يحصل ما خلق على ما خلق بل على غير ما خلق وهو أمارة اضطراره، فكان القول بما قالوا موجبا كون فعل الله تعالى بالاضطرار وهذا محال ، وإذا لم يصر العبد بتخليق فعله مضطرًا في فعله كما لم يصر بتخليق السماوات والأرض فكان خلق كفره وخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال والبحار سواء من أن لا يوجب اضطرار العبد، والله الموفق.
And the conclusion of the matter: that the denial of their creation from God Almighty is either because there is no evidence for it, or because of impossibility, or because of what the statement entails of necessitating compulsion, prevention of moral responsibility, and elevation of command and prohibition. So if their denial is due to lack of evidence, we have established scriptural and rational proofs sufficient for one who advises himself and does not argue against his intellect. And if their denial is due to impossibility, they are required to provide evidence for it, and they have no evidence except that it is inconceivable in their imaginations because a thing in their view is an act of two agents and the attachment of two powers to it, or because the statement entails association. And we have fully discharged our responsibility regarding all of that. And if their denial is due to what it entails of necessitating compulsion, we have answered that and repelled the doubts by God's praise. Moreover, the knowledge that we are choosing is established by necessity, and the establishment of choice does not negate creation. As previously stated, how can creation negate choice when He, exalted, creates the optional act, not the compulsory one, and there is a distinction between them? And the statement that He, exalted, intends to create one of them and the other occurs willingly or unwillingly is an affirmation of compulsion and incapacity for the Maker, may He be glorified and exalted. So in making the optional act compulsory due to its occurrence by creation, their denial of knowledge of necessities and making the Creator compelled in His creation is established. So if they claim that the servant cannot escape what He created, how can he be choosing? We say: The servant cannot escape God's knowledge, so how can he be choosing? And the Creator also does not escape His knowledge, so how can He be choosing? It follows from this that He, exalted, is not compelled in His act, even though He does not escape His knowledge, because even though He does not escape His knowledge, His knowledge is that He does what He does by His choice. So if He became compelled by His knowledge, He would become ignorant, since He knows that He does what He does by His choice, but He did not do it; rather, He did it compelled. Similarly, His servant does not become compelled by His knowledge, even though he does not escape His knowledge, because God's knowledge is that the servant does what he does by his choice, not compelled. So if he became compelled, God's knowledge would become ignorance, and that is impossible. Thus the servant remains choosing. Similarly, when He created by His knowledge the optional, he remains choosing; because if he thereby became compelled, what He created would not occur as He created it, but rather other than what He created, and that is a sign of his compulsion. So the statement as they said would necessitate God's act being by compulsion, and that is impossible. And if the servant does not become compelled in his act by the creation of his act, just as he does not become compelled by the creation of the heavens and the earth, then the creation of his disbelief and the creation of the heavens, the earths, the mountains, and the seas are equal in that they do not necessitate the compulsion of the servant. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument by al-Nasafi (the author) refuting the Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of human actions (takhliq) and defending human free choice (ikhtiyar). The passage is a continuous argument structured around three possible reasons for the Mu'tazila denial: lack of evidence, logical impossibility, and the necessitation of compulsion (idtirar) leading to the nullification of moral responsibility. The author first states the three possibilities. For the first, he claims sufficient scriptural and rational proofs have been given. For the second, he demands evidence from them, noting their only argument is the inconceivability of an act by two agents or the entailment of shirk, which he says he has already refuted. For the third, he asserts that human free choice is known by necessity, and that choice does not negate creation because God creates optional acts, not compulsory ones; creation of the optional does not make it compulsory. He then argues that the Mu'tazila claim that divine creation makes the act compulsory and thus the servant cannot be free is refuted by analogy with God's own knowledge: just as God is not compelled by His knowledge because His knowledge includes His free choice, so the servant is not compelled by God's creation because God's knowledge includes the servant's free choice. If the servant became compelled, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible. He concludes that the creation of an act does not necessitate compulsion, just as the creation of the heavens and earth does not compel the servant. The argument emphasizes that the same principle applies to all created things: they do not negate human free will. The passage ends with 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila denial is based on a single reason.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows there are three distinct logical possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what are the three possible reasons for denying divine creation of actions?
A) Ignorance, impossibility, compulsion
B) Lack of evidence, impossibility, compulsion
C) Lack of evidence, shirk, compulsion
D) Lack of evidence, impossibility, shirk

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila denial of God's creation of actions can be reduced to three logical possibilities, and al-Nasafi refutes the first two immediately.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you blame God for creating that action in you, or do you own it as your choice? If you truly believed He creates your actions, would your repentance be more urgent or less?
?
Is there any belief you hold simply because it 'does not make sense in your imagination,' even though God has provided clear evidence? What would it cost you to submit to His proofs instead of your imagination?
?
If you knew that denying God's role in your daily actions is itself a form of rebellion, what would you change about how you start your day or respond to your failures?

Refuting the Compulsion Objection: Knowledge and Free Choice
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that divine creation necessitates compulsion, using the principle that God creates optional acts and the analogy of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument that divine creation of actions does not negate human free choice, using the analogy of God's own knowledge.

Core Idea
Just as God's knowledge does not compel Him, His creation does not compel us.

The most powerful objection of the Mu'tazila is that if God creates all actions, humans are forced. Al-Nasafi responds with a profound analogy: God's own knowledge does not compel Him, nor does His creation compel us, because His knowledge includes our free choice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: choice is known by necessity
Al-Nasafi begins by stating that our free choice is known by necessity and that creation does not negate it, because God creates optional acts, not compulsory ones.
This is a crucial distinction: God creates the act as optional, not as forced.
2
Follow the analogy with divine knowledge
He then addresses the objection that the servant cannot escape what God created. He draws an analogy: the servant cannot escape God's knowledge either, but that does not make him compelled, because God's knowledge includes that the servant acts by choice.
If creation compelled, then God's knowledge would be wrong (ignorance), which is impossible.
3
Apply the reductio ad absurdum
If the servant were compelled by creation, then God would also be compelled by His knowledge, leading to a contradiction. Thus the servant remains choosing.
This shows the Mu'tazila objection leads to absurdity.
4
Extend the analogy to all creation
Al-Nasafi concludes by comparing the creation of human actions to the creation of the heavens and earth: just as those do not compel us, so do actions not compel us.
The creation of disbelief is no different from creation of mountains in terms of compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحصول المسألة: أن إنكار تخليقها من الله تعالى، إما أن يكون لما لا دلالة عليها أو للإحالة أو لما في القول به من إيجاب الضرورة وامتناع التكليف وارتفاع الأمر والنهي. فإن كان إنكارهم لعدم الدلالة فقد أقمنا الدلائل السمعية والعقلية ما فيه كفاية لمن نصح نفسه ولم يكابر عقله، وإن كان إنكارهم للإحالة فيطالبون بدليلها، ولا دليل معهم سوى أنه لا يتصور في أوهامهم لكون الشيء عندهم فعلاً لفاعلين وتعلق قدرتين به أو لأن القول به يوجب الشركة، وقد تقصينا عن عهدة كل ذلك وإن كان إنكارهم لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة فقد أجبنا عن ذلك ودفعنا الشبه بحمد الله، على أن العلم بكوننا مختارين ثابت بطريق الضرورة وثبوت الاختيار لا ينفي التخليق لما مر على أن التخليق كيف ينفي الاختيار وهو تعالى يخلق الفعل الاختياري لا الضروري وبينهما مفارقة، والقول بأنه تعالى يريد تخليق أحدهما فيحصل الآخر شاء أو أبى إثبات الاضطرار والعجز للصانع جل وعلا، فكان في جعل الفعل الاختياري ضروريا لحصوله بالتخليق إنكارهم علم الضروريات وجعل الباري مضطرًا في تخليقه، فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ قلنا : العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن معلوم الباري، فكيف يكون مختارا والباري لا يخرج أيضا عن معلومه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ وجاء من هذا أنه تعالى لما لم يكن في فعله مضطرًا وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأنه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه فمعلومه أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلو صار بعلمه مضطرًا لانقلب جاهلاً حيث علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره، فلم يفعل بل فعل مضطرًا فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأن معلومه تعالى أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره غير مضطر ، فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً وذلك محال، فبقى العبد مختارا فكذا لما خلق بعلمه الاختياري يبقى مختاراً ؛ إذ لو انقلب بذلك مضطرًا لم يحصل ما خلق على ما خلق بل على غير ما خلق وهو أمارة اضطراره، فكان القول بما قالوا موجبا كون فعل الله تعالى بالاضطرار وهذا محال ، وإذا لم يصر العبد بتخليق فعله مضطرًا في فعله كما لم يصر بتخليق السماوات والأرض فكان خلق كفره وخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال والبحار سواء من أن لا يوجب اضطرار العبد، والله الموفق.
And the conclusion of the matter: that the denial of their creation from God Almighty is either because there is no evidence for it, or because of impossibility, or because of what the statement entails of necessitating compulsion, prevention of moral responsibility, and elevation of command and prohibition. So if their denial is due to lack of evidence, we have established scriptural and rational proofs sufficient for one who advises himself and does not argue against his intellect. And if their denial is due to impossibility, they are required to provide evidence for it, and they have no evidence except that it is inconceivable in their imaginations because a thing in their view is an act of two agents and the attachment of two powers to it, or because the statement entails association. And we have fully discharged our responsibility regarding all of that. And if their denial is due to what it entails of necessitating compulsion, we have answered that and repelled the doubts by God's praise. Moreover, the knowledge that we are choosing is established by necessity, and the establishment of choice does not negate creation. As previously stated, how can creation negate choice when He, exalted, creates the optional act, not the compulsory one, and there is a distinction between them? And the statement that He, exalted, intends to create one of them and the other occurs willingly or unwillingly is an affirmation of compulsion and incapacity for the Maker, may He be glorified and exalted. So in making the optional act compulsory due to its occurrence by creation, their denial of knowledge of necessities and making the Creator compelled in His creation is established. So if they claim that the servant cannot escape what He created, how can he be choosing? We say: The servant cannot escape God's knowledge, so how can he be choosing? And the Creator also does not escape His knowledge, so how can He be choosing? It follows from this that He, exalted, is not compelled in His act, even though He does not escape His knowledge, because even though He does not escape His knowledge, His knowledge is that He does what He does by His choice. So if He became compelled by His knowledge, He would become ignorant, since He knows that He does what He does by His choice, but He did not do it; rather, He did it compelled. Similarly, His servant does not become compelled by His knowledge, even though he does not escape His knowledge, because God's knowledge is that the servant does what he does by his choice, not compelled. So if he became compelled, God's knowledge would become ignorance, and that is impossible. Thus the servant remains choosing. Similarly, when He created by His knowledge the optional, he remains choosing; because if he thereby became compelled, what He created would not occur as He created it, but rather other than what He created, and that is a sign of his compulsion. So the statement as they said would necessitate God's act being by compulsion, and that is impossible. And if the servant does not become compelled in his act by the creation of his act, just as he does not become compelled by the creation of the heavens and the earth, then the creation of his disbelief and the creation of the heavens, the earths, the mountains, and the seas are equal in that they do not necessitate the compulsion of the servant. And God is the Grantor of success.

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument by al-Nasafi (the author) refuting the Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of human actions (takhliq) and defending human free choice (ikhtiyar). The passage is a continuous argument structured around three possible reasons for the Mu'tazila denial: lack of evidence, logical impossibility, and the necessitation of compulsion (idtirar) leading to the nullification of moral responsibility. The author first states the three possibilities. For the first, he claims sufficient scriptural and rational proofs have been given. For the second, he demands evidence from them, noting their only argument is the inconceivability of an act by two agents or the entailment of shirk, which he says he has already refuted. For the third, he asserts that human free choice is known by necessity, and that choice does not negate creation because God creates optional acts, not compulsory ones; creation of the optional does not make it compulsory. He then argues that the Mu'tazila claim that divine creation makes the act compulsory and thus the servant cannot be free is refuted by analogy with God's own knowledge: just as God is not compelled by His knowledge because His knowledge includes His free choice, so the servant is not compelled by God's creation because God's knowledge includes the servant's free choice. If the servant became compelled, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible. He concludes that the creation of an act does not necessitate compulsion, just as the creation of the heavens and earth does not compel the servant. The argument emphasizes that the same principle applies to all created things: they do not negate human free will. The passage ends with 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine creation of actions eliminates human free will.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that creation of optional acts does not make them compulsory, just as God's knowledge does not compel Him.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi argue that divine creation does not compel the servant?
A) He says the servant has independent power over his actions.
B) He uses the analogy that just as God's knowledge does not compel Him, creation does not compel the servant.
C) He says the servant can escape God's creation.
D) He says creation is only for good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Divine creation of optional acts does not negate free choice; the same principle applies as with divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making an excuse like 'I couldn't help it' or 'God made me this way,' are you ignoring that God created that action as optional? How would you live differently today if you truly believed your actions are created as choices, not compulsions?
?
If you knew that your next sin is not a surprise to God but included in His knowledge as your choice, would you still commit it with the same ease? What does that tell you about how you view His knowledge?
?
Al-Nasafi says the creation of disbelief and the creation of mountains are equal in not compelling. Do you treat your faith as something you are forced into or as an optional act you choose daily? What specific choice will you make right now to affirm your willing submission?

The Contradiction: Punishment Without Action
Nasafi exposes the Qadari's implicit Jabri position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadari argument that humans can be free of both action and abandonment leads to the absurd conclusion that a person can be punished without performing any act, which Nasafi identifies as the very doctrine of Jabriyya.

Core Idea
Punishment without an act is the hallmark of Jabriyya.

The Qadari (Mu'tazili) opponent argues that a person with complete power can be empty of both acting and refraining, thus not coerced. Nasafi shows the absurd consequence: if someone neither prays nor abandons prayer, they should not be punished—yet the opponent claims punishment is due. This reveals that the opponent's position is exactly the Jabri view of punishing without an act, and even worse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qadari claim
The opponent claims a capable person can exit from both alternatives (action and abandonment). This is the premise that Nasafi targets.
This claim is meant to avoid coercion, but it leads to a paradox.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
If someone does nothing during prayer time, no act occurs. The opponent still says he is punished. Ask: why would he be punished if he did nothing?
3
See Nasafi's identification
Nasafi declares that punishing without an act is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya. He strengthens his point by saying the opponent's version is even worse than the Jabriyya.
This is a powerful tu quoque: the opponent ends up affirming the very position he attacks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكرها على أحدهما ، فزعم أنه يمكنه الخروج عنهما ، وزعم أن القادر السليم الآلة التام القدرة يجوز خلوه عن الفعل وتركه فكان متمكنا من الخروج عنهما، فلم يكن مكرهًا على أحدهما فارتكب المحال بذلك، ولزمه أن وقت صلاة لو انقضى وهو لم يفعل شيئًا لا فعلها ولا تركها أن لا يعاقب، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً قبيحا ومن المحال أن المأمور بالصلاة يمضي على وقت الصلاة وهو لم يصلها ولا يستحق العقاب فزعم أنه يعاقب، قيل: على ماذا يعاقب ولم يفعل فعلاً؟ فقال: يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا عين مذهب الجبرية: أن العبد يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا أربى عليهم وقال: أن هذا يجب على الله تعالى تعذيبه وجوبًا لو امتنع عن ذلك إذا خرج العبد من الدنيا قبل التوبة لصار سفيها وزالت ربوبيته، وقد رددنا هذا عليه في مسألة الاستطاعة ، وأعدنا ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا ليعرف العاقل مذهبهم الذي عليه حذاقهم في زماننا هذا، ويعلم أن نسبة هؤلاء خصومهم إلى الجبر أنهم يثبتون للعبد الفعل ويعرفون الاختيار ونفي الاضطرار بالضرورة مع أن هذه مقالتهم وقاحة عظيمة وظلم ظاهر، ثم من مذهبه في تفسير مشيئة الجبر وأن الله تعالى قادر أن يجبر العبد على الإيمان بأن يخلق له علمًا يعلم بأنه لو لم يؤمن يعذب عذابًا أليما فيصير بذلك العلم مجبوراً على الإيمان، ثم هو يزعم أن الله تعالى قادر على الظلم والكذب ولو ظلم أو كذب لاستحق الذنب وزالت ربوبيته، فصار على تقدير قوله مجبوراً على العدل والصدق؛ إذ لو لم يفعله للحقه ضرر عظيم إذ لا ضرر أعظم من زوال الألوهية وبطلان الربوبية، فكان هذا الرجل قائلاً بأنه تعالى مجبور، وقال في العباد بما هو خير محض، فلا أدرى لم ينسب غيره إلى القول بالجبر؟! والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن مثل هذه المناقضات، والتحكم على الله تعالى وعلى خلقه بالباطل والاحتجاج على الخصوم بما لا حجة فيه، والله الموفق.
He claimed that the person could exit from both [alternatives], and claimed that the capable being with sound instruments and complete power can be empty of action and its abandonment, so he was able to exit from both, thus he was not coerced into one of them, thereby committing an impossibility. And it follows from this that if the time for prayer elapses while he has done nothing—neither performed it nor abandoned it—he should not be punished, because he has not done an ugly act. But it is impossible that the one commanded to pray passes the time of prayer without praying and yet does not deserve punishment. So he claimed that he is punished. It was said: 'For what is he punished, since he has not done an act?' He said: 'He is punished not for an act that occurred from him.' This is precisely the doctrine of the Jabriyya: that the servant is punished not for an act that occurred from him. And this is even worse than them. And he said: 'It is incumbent upon God Almighty to necessarily torment him if He refrained from that, when the servant leaves the world before repentance, because then He would become foolish and His lordship would cease.' We have already refuted this in the question of istita'a (capacity). We have repeated this question here so that the intelligent person may know their doctrine, which their sharp-witted ones follow in our time, and know that the attribution of these opponents to jabr (compulsion) is that they affirm for the servant the act and recognize choice and the negation of necessity by necessity, despite the fact that this statement of theirs is great insolence and manifest injustice. Then among his doctrines is his interpretation of the will of jabr, and that God Almighty is able to compel the servant to faith by creating in him knowledge that if he did not believe he would be punished with a painful punishment, so by that knowledge he becomes compelled to faith. Then he claims that God Almighty is able to do injustice and lying, and if He did injustice or lied, He would deserve sin and His lordship would cease. So, according to his assumption, He becomes compelled to justice and truthfulness, because if He did not do them, a great harm would befall Him, and there is no greater harm than the removal of divinity and the nullification of lordship. So this man was saying that He, exalted, is compelled, and he said concerning the servants that which is pure good. So I do not know why he attributes to others the saying of jabr?! Praise be to God who protected us from such contradictions, and from arbitrarily deciding about God and His creation with falsehood, and from arguing against opponents with arguments that have no proof. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Qadari (Mu'tazili) argument that leads to the conclusion that God is compelled (majbur). Nasafi presents the opponent's reasoning: the opponent claims that a person with complete power can be free of both acting and refraining, so he cannot be coerced into one. Nasafi then shows the absurd consequence: if someone does not pray and does not abandon prayer, he would not be punished because no act occurred, but the opponent still claims he is punished—this is exactly the Jabri position (punishment without act), and even worse. The opponent also claims that God must punish the unrepentant sinner, otherwise God would become 'foolish' and lose lordship. Nasafi notes this is the same as the Jabri view. The opponent also claims God can compel faith by creating knowledge of punishment, leading to compulsion. The opponent says God could do injustice, but if He did, He would cease to be Lord, so He is compelled to justice. Thus the opponent's own reasoning implies God is compelled while denying compulsion for humans. Nasafi concludes by praising God for protection from such contradictions.

Key Terms
جبر
قادر
جبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadari argument defends human freedom and avoids Jabr.
Correction: Nasafi shows the Qadari argument leads to the very Jabri doctrine of punishing without an act, contradicting their claim of avoiding compulsion.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say is 'the exact doctrine of the Jabriyya'?
A) That God compels all actions
B) That the servant is punished not for an act that occurred from him
C) That the servant has no free will
D) That God must punish every sinner

Key Takeaway
The Qadari argument that humans can be free of both action and abandonment leads to the absurdity of punishing without an act, which is the very definition of Jabriyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your failure to perform an obligatory act (like prayer) by subtly shifting responsibility to your circumstances or even to God's decree? How does this passage expose the hidden Jabr in that excuse?
?
If you truly believe that God is just, how can you reconcile that He might punish you for something you did not do? This passage shows that such a belief is actually the doctrine of Jabriyya. Have you ever held God's justice at arm's length while secretly fearing He might be unjust?
?
The Qadari in this passage thought he was defending human free will, but ended up affirming the very thing he opposed. What deeply held beliefs do you hold that might, upon closer inspection, lead to the opposite of what you intend?

The Qadari's Doctrine Implies God is Compelled
Nasafi shows the Qadari's own views entail divine compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qadari opponent's claims about divine justice and power force the conclusion that God is compelled to act justly, contradicting their rejection of Jabr.

Core Idea
The Qadari's own logic compels God more than they claim.

Nasafi continues his refutation by examining the opponent's further claims: that God must punish the unrepentant sinner or become foolish, that God can compel faith by creating knowledge, and that God could do injustice but would lose divinity if He did. Nasafi shows that these claims imply God is compelled to act in certain ways, making the opponent a greater proponent of Jabr than those they accuse.

Guided Path
1
Examine the punishment necessity claim
The opponent says God must punish the unrepentant sinner, otherwise God would be foolish and lose lordship. Nasafi calls this a repetition of the Jabri position.
This makes punishment a divine necessity, implying compulsion.
2
Analyze the compulsion to faith
The opponent says God can compel faith by creating knowledge of punishment. Nasafi notes this explicitly describes God compelling, which the opponent condemns in others.
3
Unpack the injustice and justice claim
The opponent says God can do injustice but if He did, He would lose lordship. Therefore, God is compelled to be just and truthful, as the consequence of not being so is too great.
Nasafi points out the irony: the opponent ends up saying God is compelled (majbur).
4
See the final irony
Nasafi marvels that this opponent attributes Jabr to others while his own position forces God into compulsion. He thanks God for protection from such contradictions.
This is the climax of the refutation.

The Maturidi Doctrine of Universal Divine Will
Nasafi states the Maturidi doctrine that every originated thing occurs by God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the Maturidis, everything that occurs in creation—whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident—falls under God's eternal will.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses every created thing, without exception.

In this chapter, Imam Nasafi reaffirms the Maturidi position on divine will after having discussed its eternity and essence. He now clarifies the extent of what God wills: nothing occurs outside His will. This stands in direct contrast to the Mu'tazila, who limit divine will to only what is good and obedient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning, then the translation.
Notice the recap at the beginning referencing earlier discussions.
2
Identify the core principle
Focus on the statement 'الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى' — this is the key doctrine.
3
Observe the inclusive language
Note how Nasafi lists opposites: good/evil, beautiful/ugly, substance/accident to emphasize universality.
This indicates nothing is excluded.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider what it means for God's will to include evil and ugly things—does this imply moral approval?
The commentary explains this is about decree, not moral approval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 - الكلام في الإرادة: الفصل الرابع قد سبق منا الكلام أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية وأن المشيئة والإرادة لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد وهي صفة قائمة بذاته، وقد بينا الخلاف بيننا وبين طوائف المعتزلة فلا نعيد ونحتاج في هذا الباب إلى ذكر ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله تعالى.
إرادة الله شاملة لكل شيء]: فنقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى خيرًا كان أو (۱) شرًا، حسنًا كان أو قبيحاً ، جوهراً كان أو عرضاً ، وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى يريد من أفعالنا ما هو حكمة وطاعة ولا يريد ما هو معصية وقبيح، واختلفوا في المباحات فمنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى مريد لها، ومنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى غير مريد لها، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول البغداديين منهم فإنهم يزعمون أن الله لا يوصف بالإرادة في الحقيقة وإنما يوصف بها (۲) مجازا ، فما يقال إنه تعالى أراده فإن كان ذلك من أفعاله فمعناه أنه يفعله (۳) أو فعله، وما كان من أفغال غيره فالمراد منه أنه أمر به فلما (٤) كانت الإرادة عندهم أمرًا والمباح ليس بمأمور به فلا يكون مرادا (٥)
1 - Speech concerning will (al-irada): Chapter Four. We have already spoken that God Almighty is willing (murid) with an eternal will (irada azaliyya), and that al-mashi'a and al-irada are two terms that indicate one meaning, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have explained the disagreement between us and the sects of the Mu'tazila, so we will not repeat. And we need in this chapter to mention what properly enters under God's will. [God's will encompasses everything]: So we say: The principle with us is that every originated thing (hadith) occurs by the will of God Almighty, whether it is good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident. And the Mu'tazila claimed that God Almighty wills from our actions that which is wisdom and obedience, and does not will that which is disobedience and ugly. And they disagreed about permissible actions (mubahat): among them are those who claimed that God Almighty wills them, and among them are those who claimed that God Almighty does not will them. And this should be according to the statement of the Baghdadis among them, for they claim that God is not described by will in reality, but is described by it (2) metaphorically. So what is said that He Almighty willed it — if that is from His actions, its meaning is that He does it (3) or did it; and if it is from the actions of another, then what is intended by it is that He commanded it. Thus, since will (al-irada) according to them is command (amr), and the permissible is not commanded, it is not willed (5).
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition by Imam Nasafi on the nature of divine will, positioned as the fourth chapter of the work. It opens by reaffirming the established Maturidi position (already covered earlier) that God is 'willing' (murid) with an eternal will, that 'will' (irada) and 'volition' (mashi'a) are synonymous terms for the same attribute subsisting in God's essence, and that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila on this point has already been explained and need not be repeated. The focus of this chapter is to delineate what falls under God's will.

Nasafi then states the core principle: according to the Maturidis (and Ahl al-Sunna), every originated thing (hadith) — whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance (jawhar) or accident (arad) — occurs by God's will. This is the doctrine of universal divine will, encompassing all events including human actions.

He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position: they claim that God wills only those actions of ours that are wise and obedient, and does not will sin and ugliness. Within the Mu'tazila, there is further disagreement about permissible acts (mubahat): some say God wills them, others say He does not.

Nasafi then identifies the root of this disagreement, attributing the view that God does not will permissible acts to the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila. Their reasoning is that they deny that God has will in reality; rather, they interpret 'will' metaphorically (majazan). For them, when it is said that God willed something, if it is His own action, it means 'He does it' or 'He did it'; if it relates to another's action, it means 'He commanded it'. Since, in their view, will reduces to command (amr), and permissible acts are not commanded, they conclude that permissible acts are not willed by God.

Thus, Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila position leads to a narrow scope of divine will, limited to commanded acts, whereas the Maturidi position holds that God's will is universal and includes all events, regardless of moral or ontological category.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة
حادث
خير
شر
حسن
قبيح
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ahl al-Sunna believe God wills evil in the same sense He wills good, implying approval of evil.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that will is universal and encompasses all events, but this does not mean moral approval; it is a point of decree and creation, not moral endorsement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Maturidi position on the scope of God's will?
A) God wills only good acts.
B) God wills every originated thing, whether good or evil.
C) God wills only commanded acts.
D) God does not will human actions at all.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine asserts that God's will is all-encompassing, covering every event without exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a personal tragedy or witness evil in the world, do you truly believe that it occurred by God's will, or does your heart resist this truth? How can you train yourself to submit to His decree without resentment?
?
If God's will encompasses both good and evil, what does that mean for your own actions? Do you ever commit sins thinking they are outside God's control, or do you acknowledge that even your wrong choices occur by His will, yet you remain accountable?
?
This passage teaches that God's will includes 'beautiful and ugly' things. Consider a recent event you considered 'ugly' or unjust. How does accepting it as part of God's will change your attitude toward it? Will you still complain, or will you seek wisdom in His decree?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Refutation of Mu'tazila: The Scope of Divine Will
Nasafi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila position on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila limited divine will and why the Baghdadis among them interpreted will metaphorically, reducing it to command.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila restrict God's will to only good acts, while the Baghdadis deny real will and reduce it to command.

After stating the Maturidi principle, Nasafi now presents the Mu'tazila position and its internal divisions. He shows that their view leads to a narrow understanding of will, and particularly highlights the Baghdadis' metaphorical interpretation, which effectively denies that God has a real attribute of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the part where Nasafi describes what the Mu'tazila say about God's will regarding human actions.
2
Note their disagreement on permissible actions
Observe that the Mu'tazila themselves are divided on whether God wills mubah (permissible) acts.
This shows internal inconsistency.
3
Understand the Baghdadis' metaphorical interpretation
Focus on how the Baghdadis define 'will' as either action or command, and why this excludes permissible acts.
This is the key refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 - الكلام في الإرادة: الفصل الرابع قد سبق منا الكلام أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية وأن المشيئة والإرادة لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد وهي صفة قائمة بذاته، وقد بينا الخلاف بيننا وبين طوائف المعتزلة فلا نعيد ونحتاج في هذا الباب إلى ذكر ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله تعالى.
إرادة الله شاملة لكل شيء]: فنقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى خيرًا كان أو (۱) شرًا، حسنًا كان أو قبيحاً ، جوهراً كان أو عرضاً ، وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى يريد من أفعالنا ما هو حكمة وطاعة ولا يريد ما هو معصية وقبيح، واختلفوا في المباحات فمنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى مريد لها، ومنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى غير مريد لها، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول البغداديين منهم فإنهم يزعمون أن الله لا يوصف بالإرادة في الحقيقة وإنما يوصف بها (۲) مجازا ، فما يقال إنه تعالى أراده فإن كان ذلك من أفعاله فمعناه أنه يفعله (۳) أو فعله، وما كان من أفغال غيره فالمراد منه أنه أمر به فلما (٤) كانت الإرادة عندهم أمرًا والمباح ليس بمأمور به فلا يكون مرادا (٥)
1 - Speech concerning will (al-irada): Chapter Four. We have already spoken that God Almighty is willing (murid) with an eternal will (irada azaliyya), and that al-mashi'a and al-irada are two terms that indicate one meaning, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have explained the disagreement between us and the sects of the Mu'tazila, so we will not repeat. And we need in this chapter to mention what properly enters under God's will. [God's will encompasses everything]: So we say: The principle with us is that every originated thing (hadith) occurs by the will of God Almighty, whether it is good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident. And the Mu'tazila claimed that God Almighty wills from our actions that which is wisdom and obedience, and does not will that which is disobedience and ugly. And they disagreed about permissible actions (mubahat): among them are those who claimed that God Almighty wills them, and among them are those who claimed that God Almighty does not will them. And this should be according to the statement of the Baghdadis among them, for they claim that God is not described by will in reality, but is described by it (2) metaphorically. So what is said that He Almighty willed it — if that is from His actions, its meaning is that He does it (3) or did it; and if it is from the actions of another, then what is intended by it is that He commanded it. Thus, since will (al-irada) according to them is command (amr), and the permissible is not commanded, it is not willed (5).
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition by Imam Nasafi on the nature of divine will, positioned as the fourth chapter of the work. It opens by reaffirming the established Maturidi position (already covered earlier) that God is 'willing' (murid) with an eternal will, that 'will' (irada) and 'volition' (mashi'a) are synonymous terms for the same attribute subsisting in God's essence, and that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila on this point has already been explained and need not be repeated. The focus of this chapter is to delineate what falls under God's will.

Nasafi then states the core principle: according to the Maturidis (and Ahl al-Sunna), every originated thing (hadith) — whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance (jawhar) or accident (arad) — occurs by God's will. This is the doctrine of universal divine will, encompassing all events including human actions.

He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position: they claim that God wills only those actions of ours that are wise and obedient, and does not will sin and ugliness. Within the Mu'tazila, there is further disagreement about permissible acts (mubahat): some say God wills them, others say He does not.

Nasafi then identifies the root of this disagreement, attributing the view that God does not will permissible acts to the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila. Their reasoning is that they deny that God has will in reality; rather, they interpret 'will' metaphorically (majazan). For them, when it is said that God willed something, if it is His own action, it means 'He does it' or 'He did it'; if it relates to another's action, it means 'He commanded it'. Since, in their view, will reduces to command (amr), and permissible acts are not commanded, they conclude that permissible acts are not willed by God.

Thus, Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila position leads to a narrow scope of divine will, limited to commanded acts, whereas the Maturidi position holds that God's will is universal and includes all events, regardless of moral or ontological category.

Key Terms
معتزلة
بغداديون
مجاز
أمر
مباح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila say about God's will?
A) God wills all actions.
B) God wills only good actions.
C) God is described by will only metaphorically.
D) God wills only commanded actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila, especially the Baghdadis, reduce divine will to action or command, thereby excluding permissible acts and limiting God's volition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself saying 'God would never will that for me' about your own sins or difficulties? How does this lesson challenge your assumption about the scope of God's will?
?
If you reduce God's will to only what you consider good or wise, how might that limit your trust in His decree when you face something you deem 'ugly'? What would it mean to affirm that God wills all things, even what you don't understand?
?
The Baghdadis claimed God does not have a real will. In your daily life, do you ever act as if God's will is merely a command that you can choose to follow, rather than a real, sovereign will that encompasses everything? How does that affect your sense of accountability?

The Maturidi Principle: All Events Occur by Allah's Will
Nasafi states the Maturidi principle that everything occurs by Allah's will and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that all contingent events, whether good or evil, occur by Allah's will, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila restriction of divine will to only obedient acts.

Core Idea
Allah's will encompasses every event, including evil and disobedience.

Imam Nasafi now turns to a central Maturidi doctrine: the scope of Allah's will. In this passage, he clearly states that every contingent event—without exception—falls under the divine will. He then addresses the Mu'tazila deviation, who limit Allah's will to only acts of obedience and wisdom. This lesson lays out the Maturidi stance and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the strong opening statement and the contrast with the Mu'tazila.
The passage begins by reminding the reader of prior discussions.
2
Identify the Maturidi principle
Find the sentence beginning with 'الأصل عندنا' and understand its universal scope: every contingent event, good or evil, substance or accident, is willed by Allah.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila view
See how the Mu'tazila restrict Allah's will to acts of obedience and wisdom, and how they interpret 'will' metaphorically as command. Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for theology?
This refutes any notion that Allah does not will evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق منا الكلام أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية وأن المشيئة والإرادة لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد وهي صفة قائمة بذاته، وقد بينا الخلاف بيننا وبين طوائف المعتزلة فلا نعيد ونحتاج في هذا الباب إلى ذكر ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله تعالى. إرادة الله شاملة لكل شيء: فنقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى خيرًا كان أو شرًا، حسنًا كان أو قبيحاً ، جوهرًا كان أو عرضاً ، وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى يريد من أفعالنا ما هو حكمة وطاعة ولا يريد ما هو معصية وقبيح، واختلفوا في المباحات فمنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى مريد لها، ومنهم من زعم أن الله تعالى غير مريد لها، وينبغي أن يكون هذا على قول البغداديين منهم فإنهم يزعمون أن الله لا يوصف بالإرادة في الحقيقة وإنما يوصف بها مجازا ، فما يقال إنه تعالى أراده فإن كان ذلك من أفعاله فمعناه أنه يفعله أو فعله، وما كان من أفعال غيره فالمراد منه أنه أمر به فلما كانت الإرادة عندهم أمرًا والمباح ليس بمأمور به فلا يكون مرادا
We have previously spoken that Allah Almighty is a willer with an eternal will, and that volition (al-mashi'a) and will (al-irada) are two words that indicate one meaning, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have explained the disagreement between us and the groups of the Mu'tazila, so we will not repeat. And we need in this chapter to mention what is valid to be included under the will of Allah Almighty. [The will of Allah encompasses everything:] So we say: The principle with us is that every contingent event occurs by the will of Allah Almighty, whether it be good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident. And the Mu'tazila claimed that Allah Almighty wills from our actions only what is wisdom and obedience, and does not will what is disobedience and ugly. And they differed regarding permissible acts (al-mubahat): among them are those who claimed that Allah Almighty wills them, and among them are those who claimed that Allah Almighty does not will them. And this should be according to the statement of the Baghdadis among them, for they claim that Allah is not described with will (irada) in reality, but is described with it metaphorically. So what is said that He Almighty willed it, if it is from His actions, its meaning is that He does it or did it; and what is from the actions of others, the intended meaning is that He commanded it. Since will according to them is command, and the permissible is not commanded, so it is not willed.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the Maturidi principle that all events fall under Allah's will. Nasafi begins by reminding the reader that Allah is a willer with an eternal will, and that 'volition' (mashi'a) and 'will' (irada) are synonyms for a single attribute subsisting in His essence. After noting that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila has already been covered, he states the need to discuss what may properly be included under Allah's will. The passage then presents the Maturidi position: every contingent event (hadith) occurs by Allah's will, whether good or evil, beautiful or ugly, substance or accident. This is contrasted with the Mu'tazila view that Allah only wills acts that are wisdom and obedience, not disobedience. The Mu'tazila themselves differed about permissible acts (mubahat): some said Allah wills them, others said He does not. Nasafi links this to the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila, who deny real will to Allah and instead interpret it metaphorically: for Allah's own acts, 'will' means He does them; for others' acts, it means He commands them. Since 'will' for them means 'command', and permissible acts are not commanded, they are not willed. This refutes the Mu'tazila by showing the inconsistency of their reduction of will to command.

Misconception: That the Maturidi position means Allah approves of evil or sin.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes between will and pleasure/command; Allah wills all events but does not necessarily approve of sin.

?
The Mu'tazila limited Allah's will to what is good, perhaps to avoid attributing evil to Him. When you face a tragedy or a personal failure, do you instinctively doubt Allah's will or question His wisdom? How will you train yourself to affirm that even this is from His will, while still seeing it as a test? Pledge to recite 'Qadr Allah' sincerely in your next trial.
?
The passage distinguishes between will and command: Allah wills what He does not command. Is there an area of your life where you are following your desires despite knowing Allah's command?
