The Maturidi Distinction Between Will and Pleasure/Command
Nasafi summarizes the Maturidi view: Allah wills all events, but only commands and is pleased with obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Maturidi theology, every event occurs by Allah's will, but only obedient acts are by His command and pleasure; disobedient acts are not.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything, but only approves of obedience.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla encapsulates a critical Maturidi distinction. It clarifies that while all events fall under Allah's will and power, His command and pleasure are only associated with good acts. This safeguards divine transcendence and answers the problem of evil by not attributing evil to Allah's approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by a classification of obedience and disobedience.
Pay attention to the key terms: will, pleasure, command, decree, power.
2
Identify the classification of acts
The passage divides every event into two categories: obedience and disobedience. For each, it lists which divine attributes are involved. Note the difference in attributes between the two categories.
Obedience: involves all listed attributes. Disobedience: excludes command, pleasure, and love.
3
Understand the reason for the distinction
The passage explains that Allah's love and pleasure are based on something being 'approved' by Him. Evil is not approved, so it does not fall under these attributes. This distinction preserves divine perfection.
This is a key Maturidi principle to avoid associating evil with Allah's command or pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان (ثم ما كان) من ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئته وإرادته ورضاه ومحبته وأمره وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله تعالى وإرادته وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمر الله ولا برضاه ومحبته، وذلك لأن محبته ورضاه ترجع إلى كون الشيء عنده مستحسنًا، على هذا قدماء أصحابنا، وهو الظاهر من قول مشايخنا في ديارنا الشائع في عوامنا وخواصنا، ونص عليه شيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي
And the summary with us is that every contingent event occurs by the will of Allah Almighty, whatever description it may have. Then what among that is obedience, it is by His volition (mashi'a), His will (irada), His pleasure (rida), His love (mahabbah), His command (amr), His decree (qada'), and His power (qudra); and what is disobedience, it is by the volition of Allah Almighty, His will, His decree, and His power, but it is not by His command, nor by His pleasure and love. This is because His love and pleasure go back to something being approved (mustahsan) by Him. Upon this are our earlier companions, and it is apparent from the statement of our scholars in our land, common among our commoners and elites, and our master Abu Mansur al-Maturidi explicitly stated it.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage provides the Maturidi summary (al-hasil) of the relationship between Allah's will and His command and pleasure. It is a classification: every event, whatever its description, occurs by Allah's will. However, when the event is obedience (ta'a), it is attributed to Allah's volition, will, pleasure, love, command, decree, and power. When the event is disobedience (ma'siya), it is attributed only to volition, will, decree, and power—but not to command, pleasure, or love. The reason given is that Allah's love and pleasure refer to something being 'approved' (mustahsan) by Him; evil is not approved. Nasafi then notes that this is the position of the early Maturidi scholars, the common teaching in his region, and was explicitly stated by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi himself. This distinction safeguards divine transcendence by not associating evil with Allah's command and pleasure while affirming that all events fall under His will and power.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
مشيئة (mashi'a)
رضا (rida)
محبة (mahabbah)
أمر (amr)
قضاء (qada')
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi position equates Allah's will with His pleasure, so sinful acts are also by His command and pleasure.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes: all acts are by Allah's will, but sinful acts are not by His command, pleasure, or love. Only obedient acts are by these attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Will and power
B) Decree and volition
C) Command and pleasure
D) All attributes are associated

Key Takeaway
All events are by Allah's will, but evil is not by His command or pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah wills your every sin but does not love it, how does that change the way you view your failures? Does it make you despair of His mercy or strive harder to align your actions with what He loves?
?
You know now that your disobedience occurs by Allah's decree but not by His pleasure. When you sin, do you feel the weight of displeasing the One whose will alone brings things into existence? How can you cultivate a deeper sensitivity to His pleasure rather than merely avoiding punishment?
?
Reflect on the fact that Allah's love is reserved for what is 'approved' (mustahsan). Look at your daily life: which of your actions—public and private—would meet that approval? Are you satisfied that your heart's desires align with what He loves, or do you need to re-evaluate?

The Ash'ari View: Love and Pleasure as Will
Nasafi reports the Ash'ari claim of synonymy between love, pleasure, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that divine love and pleasure are synonymous with will and its implications for divine attitude toward all existents.

Core Idea
For al-Ash'ari, love and pleasure are not distinct from will — they are the same.

In the previous lesson, you learned the Maturidi view that divine love and pleasure are distinct from will. Here, Imam Nasafi presents the contrasting Ash'ari stance. As you read, notice how al-Ash'ari collapses these attributes into one, thereby making pleasure and love coextensive with everything Allah wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the key claim introduced by 'zama'a' (claimed).
The phrase 'bi-ma'na al-irada' is the core of the position.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Ash'ari claims that love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) are synonymous with will (irada). This means they are not separate attributes.
Note the word 'ya'muman' (they encompass) – they apply to every existent just as will does.
3
Reflect on the implication
If love and pleasure are synonymous with will, then everything Allah wills, He loves and is pleased with. This includes acts of disobedience – a major point of difference from the Maturidis.
Consider why this might be controversial: does it mean Allah loves sin?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الحسن الأشعري أن المحبة والرضى بمعنى الإرادة، ويعمان كل موجود كما تعم الإرادة فكل ما أراد وجوده فقد رضي بوجوده، وأحب وجوده على الوجه الذي أراد
And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari claimed that love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) are synonymous with will (irada), and they encompass every existent just as will encompasses [all], so everything whose existence He willed, He is pleased with its existence, and He loves its existence in the manner that He willed.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage reports the Ash'ari position in contrast to the Maturidi view. Al-Ash'ari claims that love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) are synonymous with will (irada). Consequently, they encompass every existent (mawjud) just as will encompasses all. Therefore, whatever Allah wills to exist, He is pleased with its existence and loves its existence in the manner He willed. This implies that for al-Ash'ari, there is no distinction between will and pleasure for sinful acts; if Allah wills something, He also loves and is pleased with it. This directly opposes the Maturidi distinction taught in the previous lesson.

Key Terms
محبة (mahabbah)
رضى (rida)
إرادة (irada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari's position forces the conclusion that Allah loves sin and evil in the same way He loves obedience.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that love and will are one attribute; thus, sin is willed and may be said to be loved in terms of its existence, but they often distinguish between love of existence and love of the act itself. The passage does not elaborate, but the identity claim does not necessarily entail moral approval of sin.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari as cited in the passage, how are love (mahabbah) and pleasure (rida) related to will?
A) They are distinct and opposed to will
B) They are synonymous with will
C) They only apply to obedience
D) They are metaphors

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari equates divine love and pleasure with will, so whatever Allah wills is automatically loved and pleased with.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah loves everything He wills, including the trials and hardships in your life, how does that change your attitude towards difficulties you are currently facing? Can you genuinely say you are pleased with His decree? What would it take for you to reach that acceptance?
?
The Ash'ari view equates Allah's love with His will. Does that make you feel more secure in His plan, or does it raise discomfort about the existence of evil? How do you personally reconcile His love with the suffering in the world, based on this understanding?
?
Think of a past sin you committed. According to this passage, Allah willed its existence, and thus loved it in the manner it was willed. How does this challenge your view of Allah's love and justice? What responsibility do you have to change, if His will already encompassed your action?

The Maturidi Rule: Allah's Will Follows His Knowledge
Nasafi presents the Maturidi pedagogical rule linking divine knowledge to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi rule that Allah's will is based on His knowledge of what will and will not occur, and how this distinguishes divine will from divine command.

Core Idea
Allah wills whatever He knows will exist, regardless of its moral value.

This lesson presents a key Maturidi pedagogical principle that clarifies the relationship between divine knowledge and divine will. Imam Nasafi explains that Allah's will is not based on the moral status of an act but on His foreknowledge of its occurrence. This helps resolve apparent tensions between God's commands and His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal translation. Let the author's phrasing settle before diving into interpretation.
Notice the repetition of categories: good/evil, ugly/beautiful, obedience/disobedience.
2
Understand the general rule
The passage states that whatever Allah knows will exist, He wills its existence; and whatever He knows will not exist, He wills its non-existence. This is a universal principle covering all actions.
The rule is presented as a facilitation (taysir) for learners.
3
Analyze the examples
Nasafi applies the rule to Pharaoh and Abu Jahl: Allah knew their disbelief would exist, so He willed it; He knew faith would not exist, so He willed its non-existence, even though He commanded faith and forbade disbelief.
This shows divine command and will can diverge.
4
Address the common misconception
Some might think this makes Allah the author of sin in a blameworthy sense. The passage emphasizes that will follows knowledge, not moral approval. Allah's will is based on His perfect knowledge of what will occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما علم وجوده أراد أن يوجد عند الماتريدية: ومشايخنا - رحمهم الله - يقولون تيسيراً على المتعلمين بأن ما علم الله أن يوجد أراد أراد وجوده شرًا كان أو خيرًا ، قبيحًا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية (وما علم أنه لا يكون أراد أن لا يكون شرًا كان أو خيرًا، قبيحا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية) ، فالله تعالى لما علم أنه يوجد من فرعون وأبي جهل وغيرهما من الكفرة الكفر ، أراد منهم الكفر وإن نهاهم عنه، ولما علم أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان أراد أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان وإن أمرهم به
[What He knows will exist, He wills to exist according to the Maturidis:] And our scholars – may Allah have mercy on them – say, as a facilitation for learners, that what Allah knows will exist, He wills its existence, whether it be evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience; and what He knows will not exist, He wills that it not exist, whether it be evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience. So Allah Almighty, since He knew that from Pharaoh, Abu Jahl, and other disbelievers, disbelief would exist, He willed disbelief from them, even though He forbade them from it; and since He knew that faith would not exist from them, He willed that faith not exist from them, even though He commanded them to it.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage presents a pedagogical rule (taysiran 'ala al-muta'allimin) of the Maturidi scholars linking divine knowledge to will. It states that whatever Allah knows will exist, He wills its existence, regardless of its moral value (good/evil, beautiful/ugly, obedience/disobedience). Similarly, whatever He knows will not exist, He wills its non-existence. The rule is illustrated with the examples of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl: Allah knew they would disbelieve, so He willed disbelief from them, even though He forbade them from it. Conversely, He knew they would not believe, so He willed that faith not exist from them, even though He commanded them to it. This shows that divine will follows divine knowledge, not divine command. This clarifies that Allah's command is one thing, and His will is another; what He commands may not be willed, and what He forbids may be willed.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
إرادة (will)
أمر (command)
نهي (forbiddance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this rule makes Allah the author of sin in a blameworthy sense.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that will is based on knowledge, not moral approval. Allah's will of an act does not imply He approves of it morally; His command and forbiddance remain separate.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Allah's will according to the Maturidi rule presented?
A) His command
B) His knowledge of what will exist
C) The moral value of the act
D) The act being permissible

Key Takeaway
Allah wills exactly what He knows will happen, which is why His will sometimes differs from His commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah wills even the disbelief of some people, yet commands them to believe, how does that challenge your understanding of personal accountability? Do you see your own obedience as entirely dependent on His grace, or do you still rely on your own efforts?
?
When you encounter someone committing a sin, does knowing that Allah willed that sin through His knowledge (even while forbidding it) change your reaction? Can you still sincerely dislike the sin while recognizing it as part of divine decree?
?
The passage uses Pharaoh and Abu Jahl as examples—people who were willed to disbelieve and punished for it. Does this disturb your sense of justice, or does it deepen your awe of Allah's wisdom? What does this tell you about your own small acts of obedience—are they ultimately a gift from Him?

The Mu'tazila on Command and Will
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila view linking command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that what Allah commands He wills to exist, and what He forbids He dislikes, and how this differs from the Maturidi position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila equated divine command with will, and prohibition with dislike.

Nasafi presents a key point of divergence between the Mu'tazila and Maturidi schools. The Mu'tazila assert that Allah's will directly follows His command: He wills what He commands and dislikes what He forbids. This passage lays out that claim, though it is cut short. Let's examine its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice that the passage is incomplete and ends mid-sentence.
The example about Pharaoh is missing due to page break.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage presents two claims: one about command and will, and one about prohibition and dislike. Find each.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider what this means for divine attributes. Does this view limit Allah's will? Contrast with the Maturidi position that will is independent of command.
This is a classic point of kalām debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما أمر بوجوده أراد أن يوجد عند المعتزلة: وزعمت المعتزلة أن ما أمر الله تعالى به أراد وجوده، وإن علم أنه لا يوجد وما نهى عنه كره وجوده، وأراد أن لا يوجد وإن علم وجوده فلما كان أمر فرعون
[What He commands to exist, He wills to exist according to the Mu'tazila:] And the Mu'tazila claimed that what Allah Almighty commands, He wills its existence, even if He knows it will not exist; and what He forbids, He dislikes its existence, and wills that it not exist, even if He knows it will exist. So when the command of Pharaoh... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage reports the Mu'tazila position in contrast to the Maturidi view. The Mu'tazila claim that whatever Allah commands, He wills its existence, regardless of whether He knows it will actually occur. Conversely, whatever He forbids, He dislikes (yakrah) its existence and wills that it not exist, even if He knows it will exist. This reduces divine will to divine command: if Allah commands something, He wills it; if He forbids something, He does not will it and dislikes it. The text begins to provide an example concerning Pharaoh (فرعون) but breaks off due to the page ending. This view opposes the Maturidi principle that will follows knowledge, not command.

Key Terms
أمر (amr)
نهى (nahy)
كره (dislike)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny Allah's will over sinful acts
Correction: They do not deny it; they redefine it. They say Allah does not will sin because He forbids it, so He dislikes it and wills its non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view reported, what is the relationship between command and will?
A) Command and will are independent
B) What Allah commands, He wills to exist
C) What Allah commands, He wills not to exist
D) Command is the same as dislike

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila held that Allah's will is expressed in His commands and prohibitions, so He wills what He commands and dislikes what He forbids.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believed that every command God gives you is a sign that He wills it for you personally, how would that change the way you respond to His commands in moments of weakness? Would it increase your urgency or burden you with guilt?
?
The Mu'tazila say God wills what He commands even if it never happens. Does this idea make God's will seem ineffective or inconsistent to you? Examine your discomfort—what does it reveal about your own assumptions regarding God's power and will?
?
When you fail to obey a command, do you tend to think that God's will was not really for you to do it? How does the Ahl al-Sunna position—that God may command what He does not will—either comfort or challenge your personal accountability?

The Maturidi Distinction Between Divine Will and Pleasure
Nasafi states the Maturidi distinction between divine will and divine pleasure regarding good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis distinguish between divine will (irādah) on one hand, and divine love (maḥabbah) and pleasure (riḍā) on the other, particularly regarding good and evil actions.

Core Idea
God wills all events, but He loves and takes pleasure only in what is good.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi articulates a key Maturidi theological distinction: while everything that occurs—whether obedience or disobedience—happens by God's will, His love and pleasure are not coextensive with His will. Only good acts are objects of divine love and pleasure, whereas evil acts are willed but not loved. This sets the Maturidi view apart from the Ash'ari equation of love/pleasure with will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall claim.
Notice the contrast between 'obedience' and 'disobedience' categories.
2
Identify the two categories of acts
Look for what attributes are listed for acts of obedience (ṭāʿah) and what for acts of disobedience (maʿṣiyah). Note which attributes are common and which are exclusive.
The key distinction: love and pleasure are only for obedience.
3
Understand the reason given
Find the sentence explaining why love and pleasure are not for disobedience. It says they refer to something being 'considered good' (mustaḥsanan) in God's sight.
This is the core of the Maturidi position.
4
Contrast with Ash'ari view
Nasafi presents the Ash'ari position that love and pleasure are synonymous with will. Compare that with the Maturidi view.
The Ash'ari view makes love coextensive with will, eliminating the moral distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان [ثم ما كان؟] من ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئته وإرادته ورضاه ومحبته وأمره وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله تعالى وإرادته وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمر الله ولا برضاه ومحبته، وذلك لأن محبته ورضاه ترجع إلى كون الشيء عنده مستحسنًا، على هذا قدماء أصحابنا، وهو الظاهر من قول مشايخنا في ديارنا الشائع في عوامنا وخواصنا، ونص عليه شيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي. وزعم أبو الحسن الأشعري أن المحبة والرضى بمعنى الإرادة، ويعمان كل موجود كما تعم الإرادة فكل ما أراد وجوده فقد رضي بوجوده، وأحب وجوده على الوجه الذي أراد.
And the conclusion with us is that every event occurs by the will of Allah most high, whatever description it was. Then whatever of that is obedience, it is by His wish, His will, His pleasure, His love, His command, His decree, and His power. And whatever is disobedience, it is by the wish of Allah most high, His will, His decree, and His power, and it is neither by His command nor by His pleasure nor His love. That is because His love and His pleasure refer to the thing being considered good in His sight. Upon this (are) our early companions, and it is the apparent from the saying of our shaykhs in our land, common among our commoners and elites, and our shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi explicitly stated it. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari claimed that love and pleasure are in the sense of will, and they encompass every existent as will encompasses, so everything whose existence He willed, He has been pleased with its existence and loved its existence in the manner in which He willed.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and comparison. Nasafi first states the Maturidi position: every contingent event (hādith) occurs by the will of Allah, but acts of obedience (ṭāʿah) also involve His pleasure (riḍā), love (maḥabbah), command (amr), and decree (qaḍāʾ wa qadar), whereas acts of disobedience (maʿṣiyah) occur only by His will and decree but not by His command, pleasure, or love. The reason given is that Allah's love and pleasure refer to something being considered good (mustaḥsanan) in His sight; He does not love or take pleasure in evil. Nasafi then reports that the early Maturidi masters held this view, and it was explicitly stated by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He then contrasts with Abu al-Hasan al-Ashʿari, who claimed that love and pleasure are synonymous with will (irādah), so that everything Allah wills to exist, He also loves and takes pleasure in, on the specific manner in which He willed it. Thus the Maturidi position distinguishes between the scope of will (comprehensive) and the scope of love/pleasure (restricted to what is good in His sight), unlike the Ashʿari equation.

Key Terms
إرادة
محبة
رضا
طاعة
معصية
مشيئة
قضاء
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis deny that God wills evil acts.
Correction: Maturidis affirm that God wills all events, including evil, but He does not love or command evil.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position as stated by Nasafi, what is the relationship of divine love to acts of disobedience?
A) Allah loves disobedience because He wills it.
B) Allah does not love disobedience; He wills it but does not love it.
C) Allah's love for disobedience is identical to His will.
D) Allah loves disobedience only if it is commanded.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school distinguishes divine will from divine love and pleasure: God wills all events but only loves and takes pleasure in what is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not take pleasure in your disobedience, even though He wills its occurrence, how does that make you feel about persisting in sin? What will you do today to turn your heart toward what He loves?
?
The Maturidi view holds that God's love is tied to what is good in His sight. Examine your own actions: are you more focused on what God loves or on fulfilling your own desires? Name one specific habit you will change to seek His pleasure over your own wants.
?
This lesson shows that even within the same theological tradition, there is a sharp distinction between will and love. How does knowing that God does not love disobedience give you both hope and urgency? Write a short personal commitment to avoid one sin because it displeases Him.

Maturidi Principle: Divine Will Matches Knowledge
The author states the Maturidi rule that divine will aligns with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that Allah's will corresponds to His knowledge, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila view that will follows command.

Core Idea
Allah's will matches His knowledge, not His commands.

This passage lays the foundation for the Maturidi doctrine of divine will. The author contrasts the Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions, stating that for the Maturidis, will aligns with knowledge, while for the Mu'tazila it aligns with command. This principle is crucial for understanding how the school addresses the problem of evil and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow from examples to the principle.
2
Identify the key contrast
Find the sentence that contrasts the Maturidi and Mu'tazila positions. Understand that for the Maturidis, divine will is determined by knowledge, not by command.
The text says: 'فكانت إرادة الله عندنا موافقة للعلم وعندهم موافقة للأمر'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this distinction matters. How does it affect the understanding of Allah's will for evil acts?
This principle allows the Maturidis to say that Allah wills everything He knows will occur, including evil, without implying He commands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإيمان أراد منه الإيمان وإن علم أنه لا يوجد منه، ولما نهاه عن الكفر أراد أن لا يوجد منه الكفر وإن علم أنه يوجد فكانت إرادة الله عندنا موافقة للعلم وعندهم موافقة للأمر، وعلى هذا يدور الخلاف. ثم اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا في ذلك فمنهم من قال : أقول في الجملة: إن الله تعالی مرید حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ولا ينتفي عنه ما أراد كما أطلقه المسلمون بقولهم : ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن قال: ولا أقول بالتفصيل أنه أراد الكفر والفرية عليه وشتم نفسه وغير ذلك من المعاصي، كما يقال إنه خالق العالم، ولا يقال على التفصيل أنه خالق الأقذار والأنتان والشياطين والعقارب، وإن كانت هذه التفاصيل داخلة تحت الجملة فكذا هذا، وهذا كما يقال كل ما سوى الله ضعيف على الجملة، ولا يقال دين الله ضعيف على التفصيل، ويقال : كل مخلوق حجة الله تعالى ويقال : إن هذه الخشبة منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم.
He intended from him faith, even though He knows it will not occur from him. And when He forbade him from disbelief, He intended that disbelief not occur from him, even though He knows it will occur. So the will of Allah, according to us, is concurrent with knowledge, and according to them (the Mu'tazila) it is concurrent with command. Upon this revolves the disagreement. Then the expressions of our companions differed concerning that. Among them is one who said: I say in general: Allah is willing the occurrence of everything He knows will occur. Nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills to be, and nothing He wills is negated from Him, as the Muslims have expressed in their saying: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' He said: I do not say in detail that He willed disbelief, slander against Him, reviling His own self, and other acts of disobedience, just as it is said that He is the Creator of the world, but it is not said in detail that He is the Creator of filth, foul odors, devils, and scorpions, even though these details are included under the general statement. So also this. And this is like saying: Everything other than Allah is weak in general, but one does not say: The religion of Allah is weak in detail. And it is said: Every creature is a proof of Allah, and it is said: This piece of wood is broken, but it is not said: The proof of Allah is broken. And it is said: The night is dark, but it is not said: The proof of Allah is dark. So likewise is this. And this is the view of the early scholars among our companions, among them Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. And among them is one who says: I do not confine myself in detail to saying that Allah willed disbelief and fornication; rather I add a qualification to it, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as his acquisition, evil from him, blameworthy, and similarly in other acts of disobedience, so that it does not lead to the misunderstanding of error. This is the choice of al-Ash'ari, and it is close to what our teacher Abu Mansur chose. And there is no disagreement among our companions concerning the issue in reality; rather some choose an expression from which there does not occur to the mind of a listener a meaning the speaker did not intend.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on divine will in relation to evil acts. The author first states the fundamental principle: Allah's will corresponds to His knowledge, not to His command, contrary to the Mu'tazila who hold that will must follow command. Then he presents two approaches among Maturidi scholars on how to express that Allah wills evil. The first approach, held by early scholars like Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, is to affirm divine will for all things in general terms—such as saying 'Whatever Allah wills happens'—but to refrain from explicitly stating in detail that Allah wills specific sins like disbelief or slander. They draw analogies: just as we say Allah is the Creator of the world generally but not of filth in detail, so we speak of divine will. The second approach, attributed to al-Ash'ari and close to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, allows detailed mention but with a qualification (qarina) that clarifies that the act is bad and blameworthy from the creature's side, to avoid misunderstanding. The author concludes that there is no real difference in doctrine, only in choice of phrasing to avoid unintended implications.

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
أمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position stated in this passage, divine will is concurrent with what?
A) Command (al-amr)
B) Knowledge (al-ilm)
C) Human will
D) Divine power

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school defines divine will as aligning with knowledge, not command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah wills something from you that He knows will never happen, does that shake your trust in His wisdom? How do you reconcile your free choices with His all-encompassing will?
?
When you sin, do you secretly think that Allah must have willed it for it to have happened? How does this lesson change the way you take responsibility for your actions?
?
The Mu'tazila believed that Allah only wills what He commands, making His will always righteous by human standards. Does it bother you that Allah may will things that seem unjust? How does the Maturidi view challenge you to submit to His knowledge over your own understanding?

Two Maturidi Ways to Express Divine Will for Evil
Two Maturidi methods for expressing divine will regarding evil acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approved Maturidi approaches to stating that Allah wills evil acts: the general statement without detail, and the detailed statement with a qualification (qarina).

Core Idea
Maturidis agree on the reality but differ on how to phrase divine will for evil.

After establishing the principle, the author presents two ways Maturidi scholars express divine will regarding evil. The first avoids specifying details, using only general affirmations. The second permits detailed mention but adds a clarifying qualification. Both are valid and reflect careful theological caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic text and its translation. Notice the two views introduced with 'فمنهم من قال' and 'ومنهم من يقول'.
2
Identify the first approach
Find the view that uses only general statements (jimla) and avoids explicit mention of evil acts. Note the analogies provided (e.g., creator of filth, broken proof).
This is the view of early Maturidis like Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan.
3
Identify the second approach
Find the view that allows detailed mention but with a qualification (qarina) that clarifies the evil nature of the act from the creature's perspective.
This is attributed to al-Ash'ari and close to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence where the author states there is no real disagreement, only differences in expression to avoid misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإيمان أراد منه الإيمان وإن علم أنه لا يوجد منه، ولما نهاه عن الكفر أراد أن لا يوجد منه الكفر وإن علم أنه يوجد فكانت إرادة الله عندنا موافقة للعلم وعندهم موافقة للأمر، وعلى هذا يدور الخلاف. ثم اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا في ذلك فمنهم من قال : أقول في الجملة: إن الله تعالی مرید حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ولا ينتفي عنه ما أراد كما أطلقه المسلمون بقولهم : ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن قال: ولا أقول بالتفصيل أنه أراد الكفر والفرية عليه وشتم نفسه وغير ذلك من المعاصي، كما يقال إنه خالق العالم، ولا يقال على التفصيل أنه خالق الأقذار والأنتان والشياطين والعقارب، وإن كانت هذه التفاصيل داخلة تحت الجملة فكذا هذا، وهذا كما يقال كل ما سوى الله ضعيف على الجملة، ولا يقال دين الله ضعيف على التفصيل، ويقال : كل مخلوق حجة الله تعالى ويقال : إن هذه الخشبة منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم.
He intended from him faith, even though He knows it will not occur from him. And when He forbade him from disbelief, He intended that disbelief not occur from him, even though He knows it will occur. So the will of Allah, according to us, is concurrent with knowledge, and according to them (the Mu'tazila) it is concurrent with command. Upon this revolves the disagreement. Then the expressions of our companions differed concerning that. Among them is one who said: I say in general: Allah is willing the occurrence of everything He knows will occur. Nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills to be, and nothing He wills is negated from Him, as the Muslims have expressed in their saying: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' He said: I do not say in detail that He willed disbelief, slander against Him, reviling His own self, and other acts of disobedience, just as it is said that He is the Creator of the world, but it is not said in detail that He is the Creator of filth, foul odors, devils, and scorpions, even though these details are included under the general statement. So also this. And this is like saying: Everything other than Allah is weak in general, but one does not say: The religion of Allah is weak in detail. And it is said: Every creature is a proof of Allah, and it is said: This piece of wood is broken, but it is not said: The proof of Allah is broken. And it is said: The night is dark, but it is not said: The proof of Allah is dark. So likewise is this. And this is the view of the early scholars among our companions, among them Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. And among them is one who says: I do not confine myself in detail to saying that Allah willed disbelief and fornication; rather I add a qualification to it, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as his acquisition, evil from him, blameworthy, and similarly in other acts of disobedience, so that it does not lead to the misunderstanding of error. This is the choice of al-Ash'ari, and it is close to what our teacher Abu Mansur chose. And there is no disagreement among our companions concerning the issue in reality; rather some choose an expression from which there does not occur to the mind of a listener a meaning the speaker did not intend.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on divine will in relation to evil acts. The author first states the fundamental principle: Allah's will corresponds to His knowledge, not to His command, contrary to the Mu'tazila who hold that will must follow command. Then he presents two approaches among Maturidi scholars on how to express that Allah wills evil. The first approach, held by early scholars like Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, is to affirm divine will for all things in general terms—such as saying 'Whatever Allah wills happens'—but to refrain from explicitly stating in detail that Allah wills specific sins like disbelief or slander. They draw analogies: just as we say Allah is the Creator of the world generally but not of filth in detail, so we speak of divine will. The second approach, attributed to al-Ash'ari and close to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, allows detailed mention but with a qualification (qarina) that clarifies that the act is bad and blameworthy from the creature's side, to avoid misunderstanding. The author concludes that there is no real difference in doctrine, only in choice of phrasing to avoid unintended implications.

Key Terms
جملة
تفصيل
قرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two Maturidi views on divine will for evil are contradictory.
Correction: The author explicitly states: 'ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة' meaning there is no disagreement in reality, only a difference in phrasing to avoid misunderstanding.

Checkpoint
What does the second approach add to the detailed statement about divine will for evil acts?
A) A negation that Allah wills evil
B) A qualification (qarina) clarifying the act's evil from the creature's side
C) A comparison to creation of filth
D) An emphasis on divine power

Key Takeaway
Maturidis either speak generally or add a qualification to avoid misinterpretation when detailing divine will for evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness or experience evil in the world, do you instinctively attribute it to Allah's will in a general way, or do you find yourself questioning His justice in the details? How might the Maturidi qualification— 'He willed it as an evil acquisition from the creature'— protect you from falling into despair or misjudgment of Allah? Write down one concrete situation where you will apply this mindset.
?
The passage warns against expressions that could imply error in Allah, even if the meaning is correct. Are there phrases you commonly use (e.g., 'Allah put this trial in my life') that might unintentionally suggest Allah does evil directly? What will you change in your speech today to ensure you never misrepresent His perfection, even in casual conversation?

Two Approaches to Answering About Divine Will
Maturidi presents two ways to answer about divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approaches a theologian should take when asked about divine will: speaking unrestrictedly or refraining based on the questioner's intent.

Core Idea
Knowing when to speak and when to pause is essential in theological discussion.

Abu Mansur al-Maturidi provides a practical method for handling questions about divine will. He distinguishes between two scenarios: when the questioner's intent is clear and one can reply based on the common understanding of 'will', and when the intent is unclear or contentious, requiring a more precise answer. This lesson introduces that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two aspects
Read the Arabic passage that introduces 'two aspects' of answering. Notice the conditions for each approach.
The second aspect involves a deeper explanation of the word 'mashi'a'.
2
Understand the first approach
The first approach is to speak unrestrictedly based on the understood meaning of will (al-irada). This is suitable when the questioner's intent is known and there is no fear of argument.
This assumes a shared understanding of the term.
3
Understand the second approach
The second approach is to refrain from speaking generally if the questioner's intent is unclear or if one fears contentiousness. This leads to a detailed clarification of the term 'mashi'a'.
This approach is more cautious and thorough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن: يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ.
Then our teacher Abu Mansur said: If we are asked about this question, it has two aspects: one is to speak unrestrictedly according to the understood meaning of will (al-irada). The second is to refrain from speaking unrestrictedly if the questioner's intent is not understood or one fears contentiousness. In it is that it is said: Indeed, 'mashi'a' (will) has meanings according to common usage: one is for wishful thinking (al-tamanni), the second is command and invitation to it, the third is approval of it, and the fourth is the negation of being overcome and the act's occurrence according to what He determines and wills. The first is negated in every thing, because wishful thinking is willing what one knows will not happen—which is weakness—or willing what one doubts will happen—which is ignorance. The second and third are attributes for every act that is blamed. The fourth is what we affirm, and we and those who disagree with us have agreed on its meaning even if we differ in wording.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed answer of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi to the question of how to speak about divine will. He says there are two approaches: either to speak in a general way based on the understood meaning of will, or to avoid general statements if the questioner's intent is unclear or if one fears contentiousness. He then explains that the word 'mashi'a' (will) has four conventional meanings: (1) wishful thinking (tamanni) – wanting something impossible or uncertain, which implies weakness or ignorance; (2) command and invitation; (3) approval; (4) negation of being overcome and the actual determination of an event. The first meaning is negated regarding Allah. The second and third are attributes of acts that are blameworthy. Only the fourth meaning is what Maturidis affirm—that Allah's will means He decrees and brings about events without being overcome. Abu Mansur notes that both Maturidis and their opponents agree on this meaning, though they differ on the wording.

Key Terms
مشيئة
إرادة
الإطلاق
منع الإطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any meaning of 'will' can be applied to Allah without distinction.
Correction: The passage shows that the proper response depends on the questioner's intent; a general answer may be given if the common understanding of will is meant, but a nuanced answer is needed otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, when should one refrain from speaking unrestrictedly about divine will?
A) When the questioner is knowledgeable
B) When the questioner's intent is unclear or one fears contentiousness
C) When the question is asked in private
D) When the answer is brief

Key Takeaway
Theologians must discern when to give a general answer and when to provide a detailed clarification about divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a question about Allah's will that you cannot explain fully, will you resort to a simplistic answer that might be misunderstood, or will you humbly admit the need for clarification? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to oversimplify or to remain silent out of pride? Commit now to pausing and seeking clarity before speaking about Allah's attributes.
?
Have you ever been more concerned with being right than with making your words understood accurately? The second approach warns about contentiousness. When you discuss Allah's will with others, is your intention to guide or to win an argument? What specific situation in your life requires you to choose the second approach today?
?
The four meanings of 'mashi'a' show that even a single word can have multiple valid interpretations. How often do you assume you know exactly what someone means when they speak about Allah? This lesson calls you to ask clarifying questions rather than jumping to conclusions. Will you commit to doing that in your next conversation about divine will?

The Four Meanings of 'Mashi'a' and Their Theological Application
Maturidi evaluates the four meanings of 'mashi'a' theologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conventional meanings of 'mashi'a' (will) and why only the fourth meaning—sovereign determination—applies to Allah.

Core Idea
Only one meaning of 'will' befits Allah: that He decrees and brings about all things.

After introducing the two approaches, Abu Mansur explains that the word 'mashi'a' (will) has four distinct meanings in common usage. He then evaluates each meaning theologically: wishful thinking, command, approval, and sovereign determination. This lesson focuses on that evaluation, showing why only the fourth meaning is affirmed for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the theological evaluation of the four meanings
Read the Arabic passage that explains why the first three meanings cannot apply to Allah and why the fourth is affirmed.
Pay attention to the reasons given: weakness, ignorance, blame.
2
Understand the negation of the first meaning
The first meaning (wishful thinking) is negated because it implies weakness or ignorance, which are impossible for Allah.
Wishful thinking involves wanting what cannot happen or doubting.
3
Understand the negation of the second and third meanings
The second (command) and third (approval) are attributes of blameworthy acts, so they cannot be attributed to Allah in the context of sinful acts.
Allah's command and approval are only for good, but as a general meaning, they are not directly applied.
4
Affirm the fourth meaning
The fourth meaning—negation of being overcome and the actual determination of events—is what Maturidis and their opponents agree upon as the correct meaning of divine will.
This meaning emphasizes Allah's absolute power and decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن: يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ.

Checkpoint

A) Because it implies weakness or ignorance
B) Because it is a synonym for command

The Identity of Divine Will and Creation of Human Actions
Author states the identity of two theological issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi tradition treats the issue of divine will for all things as inseparable from the creation of human actions.

Core Idea
Divine will and creation of actions are the same theological question at root.

This lesson unpacks a dense statement where the author declares that the entire discussion about divine will for evil and human actions is really one and the same as the issue of the creation of actions. Though the text cuts off, the identity claim is clear and foundational. We'll examine what this means and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice that the author states an identity: 'this question is in reality identical to the question of the creation of actions.'
The passage is incomplete; the author's full argument continues beyond this excerpt.
2
Understand the identity claim
Consider what it means for two theological issues to be 'identical in reality.' The author is asserting that the problem of divine will for evil and the problem of God creating human actions are not separate; they are the same inquiry from different angles.
3
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
If divine will and creation of actions are identical, then affirming that Allah wills all things (including disbelief and sin) directly implies that Allah creates those actions. This is a key Maturidi position against those who separate the two.
This connects to debates with the Mu'tazila and others who denied that Allah creates evil actions.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Review the common misunderstanding: some may treat divine will and creation of actions as separate topics. The author explicitly rejects that separation and unifies them.
The checkpoint question tests this understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذه المسألة في الحقيقة عين مسألة خلق الأفعال فإنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن الله تعالى خالق أفعال العباد كفرًا كان أو معصية أو طاعة ، والله تعالى مختار في
Then this question is in reality identical to the question of the creation of actions. For when we prove by evidence that Allah is the Creator of the actions of servants, whether it be disbelief, disobedience, or obedience, and Allah is free in...
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage is a brief but significant statement: the author declares that the entire preceding discussion about divine will for evil and the nuances of expression is ultimately the same as the theological issue of the creation of human actions (khalk al-af'al). He begins to state that once we prove that Allah creates all human actions—whether disbelief, sin, or obedience—then it follows that... The argument is cut off, but the connection is clear. The Maturidi position is that affirming divine will for all things is inseparable from affirming that Allah creates all actions, including those that are morally evil from the human perspective.

Key Terms

خالق
كسب
طاعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some treat the issues of divine will and creation of actions as separate theological questions.
Correction: The author asserts they are 'in reality identical' ('عين مسألة خلق الأفعال'), meaning they are fundamentally the same issue.

Checkpoint

A) They are unrelated
B) They are fundamentally the same issue
C) The question of will is more important
D) The question of creation is easier to prove

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi tradition insists that affirming divine will for all things necessarily includes affirming that Allah creates all human actions, making the two issues identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proper Expression for God's Will for Evil
Nasafi presents views on expressing God's will for evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly positions on how to properly express that Allah wills evil actions, including the views of early Maturidis, al-Ash'ari, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
When speaking of God's will for evil, qualify the statement to avoid misunderstanding.

Theologians faced a challenge: how can we say God wills disbelief and sin without implying that He approves of evil? Scholars developed careful expressions to avoid misinterpretation. This lesson presents the key approaches from early Maturidi scholars, al-Ash'ari, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, showing that the differences are only in phrasing, not in doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogies
Start by reading the passage and noting the analogies given: 'Allah's proof is not broken' and 'the night is dark'. Why are these analogies used?
The analogies illustrate how certain phrases can be misleading even if technically true.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents the view of early Maturidis (like 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan) who avoid the unrestricted statement, and the view of al-Ash'ari who adds a qualifier (kash). Then it mentions that Abu Mansur's choice is close to al-Ash'ari's. Find each position in the text.
3
Understand the underlying agreement
Notice that the author says there is no real disagreement; the difference is only in the choice of words. Reflect on why caution in expression matters in theology.
The goal is to prevent a hearer from misunderstanding that God 'errs' or approves of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم. ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن : يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ.
It is not broken, and it is not said 'Allah's proof is broken', and it is said 'the night is dark', and it is not said 'Allah's proof is dark', so likewise this. And this is the view of the early companions of ours, among them 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan. And among them is one who says: I do not confine myself in detail to saying that Allah willed disbelief and adultery, rather I add a qualifier, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as an acquisition (kash) from him, ugly from him, blameworthy, and similarly in other sins, lest it lead to implying error. And this is the choice of al-Ash'ari, and it is close to what our teacher Abu Mansur chose. And there is no disagreement among our companions in the matter in reality, rather some choose an expression from which no meaning that the speaker did not intend precedes to the imagination of a hearer. Then our teacher Abu Mansur said: If we are asked about this matter, it has two aspects: one is the unrestricted application based on the understood meaning of will (al-irada). The second is the prevention of unrestricted application if the intention of the questioner is not understood or [if] one fears obstinacy in it. And it is that it is said: Indeed, the term 'mashi'a' has meanings in common usage: one of them is for wishing (al-tamanni), the second is command and invitation to it, the third is approval (al-rida) with it, and the fourth is negation of being overcome and the act's issuing forth in accordance with what He determines and wills. The first is negated in everything, because wishing is willing what one knows will not be, and it is weakness, or willing what one doubts its occurrence, and it is ignorance. The second and third are attributes in every action for which one is blamed. The fourth is what we say, and we and those who oppose us have agreed upon its meaning, even if we differ in wording.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage defines the correct expression regarding God's will for evil actions and explains the meanings of the term mashi'a. It begins by giving analogies: just as one does not say 'Allah's proof is broken' or 'Allah's proof is dark', one should not say without qualification that Allah willed disbelief and adultery, because of potential misunderstanding. Then it mentions that early scholars like 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan held this view. Others, like al-Ash'ari, say they do not simply say 'Allah willed disbelief' but add a qualifier that He willed it as an acquisition (kash) from the servant, ugly and blameworthy, to avoid implying error. The author (al-Nasafi) says there is no real disagreement among the scholars; they differ only in expression. Then he quotes Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) who says the issue has two aspects: (1) using the unrestricted phrase based on the understood meaning of irada (will) – since God wills everything that occurs, including evil; (2) avoiding the unrestricted phrase if the questioner misunderstands or if one fears obstinacy. Then Abu Mansur explains that the word mashi'a (will) has four conventional meanings: (a) wishing (tamanni) – which is negated of God because it involves weakness or ignorance; (b) command and invitation – which along with (c) approval (rida) are blameworthy attributes when applied to evil acts; and (d) the meaning of negation of being overcome and causing the act to occur according to His determination and will – this is the meaning that all parties agree upon, even if they differ in wording. The passage thus clarifies the semantic range of 'will' and the proper way to speak about God's will regarding evil.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'Allah willed disbelief' is always wrong or that the scholars disagree on whether Allah wills evil.
Correction: The passage shows that all scholars agree that Allah wills everything that occurs, including evil, but they differ only in the phrasing used to express this to avoid misinterpretation.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari add the qualifier 'as an acquisition (kash)' when saying that Allah wills disbelief?
A) To indicate that Allah does not really will it
B) To avoid implying that Allah is the direct doer of the evil
C) To make it clear that the act is ugly and blameworthy from the servant
D) To show that the act is not part of Allah's decree

Key Takeaway
Scholars agree that God wills all things, including evil, but caution in expression is needed to avoid implying error or approval of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about events in your life that seem evil or unjust, do you ever attribute them to God without qualification? How might your words lead others to misunderstand God's justice? What will you change in your speech today to avoid that?
?
The scholars differed on expression but agreed on the truth. Are you more concerned with correct wording or correct understanding? In your own discussions about divine decree, do you prioritize precision to avoid leading others astray, even if it feels awkward?
?
If a non-Muslim asks you why God allows evil, how would you answer based on this lesson? Are you prepared to speak with both truth and caution, or do you risk misrepresenting God? Commit to preparing a response that includes the qualifier from al-Ash'ari.

The Four Meanings of Mashi'a (Will)
Abu Mansur lists the four meanings of mashi'a and identifies the agreed one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conventional meanings of mashi'a (will) as explained by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and which meaning is agreed upon by all parties in discussions about divine will.

Core Idea
Mashi'a has four meanings; only one applies to God without blame.

The Arabic word mashi'a (will) is used in multiple senses in common language. Abu Mansur al-Maturidi distinguishes four of these meanings to clarify theological discussion about God's will. This lesson unpacks each meaning, explains why three are not attributed to God, and identifies the one that all parties—Maturidis and their opponents—agree upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of four meanings
Read the passage where Abu Mansur lists the four meanings: al-tamanni (wishing), al-amr (command), al-rida (approval), and nafy al-ghalaba (negation of being overcome).
Note that these are conventional meanings in common usage, not technical theological definitions.
2
Understand why the first three are negated of Allah
The passage explains that tamanni is negated because it implies weakness or ignorance. The second and third (command and approval) are attributes of actions for which one is blamed—so they cannot be applied to God's will for evil acts. Reflect on why.
God does not 'wish' for things He knows cannot happen, nor does He 'command' sin, nor does He 'approve' of evil.
3
Identify the agreed meaning
The fourth meaning—negation of being overcome and causing the act to occur according to His determination—is the one that Maturidis and their opponents agree upon, even if they differ in wording. This is the core meaning of divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منكسرة ولا يقال حجة الله منكسرة، ويقال : الليلة مظلمة، ولا يقال حجة الله مظلمة فكذا هذا، وإليه ذهب قدماء أصحابنا منهم عبد الله بن سعيد القطان. ومنهم من يقول : لا أقتصر عند التفصيل أن أقول: إن الله تعالى أراد الكفر والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبًا له قبيحًا منه مذموماً وكذا في غيره من المعاصي لئلا يؤدي إلى إيهام الخطأ وهو اختيار الأشعري وهو قريب مما اختاره شيخنا أبو منصور، ولا خلاف بين أصحابنا في المسألة في الحقيقة بل يختار البعض عبارة لا يسبق منها إلى وهم سامع معنى لم يقصده المتكلم. ثم قال شيخنا أبو منصور : إذا سئلنا عن هذه المسألة فلها وجهان: أحدهما الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة. والثاني: منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي التعنت، فيه وهو أن : يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف أحدها للتمني، والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، والثالث الرضا به، والرابع نفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده. فالأول منفي في كل شيء، لأن التمني إرادة ما علم أنه لا يكون وهو ضعف أو إرادة ما شك في كونه وهو جهل. والثاني والثالث صفتان في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع به نقول، وقد أجمعنا نحن ومن خالفنا على معناه وإن اختلفنا في اللفظ.

?

?

Since God's will is free from tamanni, how does this strengthen your trust that what He wills for you is always better—even when you don't understand it? Commit to saying 'Alhamdulillah' for both good and bad situations for the next seven days.
?
If God's will for evil means He is not overcome and brings it about according to His plan, does that make you uncomfortable? Are you willing to accept that God's wisdom in allowing evil is beyond your comprehension? Pray to Him tonight, asking for firm belief in His perfect will, even when you cannot see the good in it.

Divine Will and Creation of Actions: One and the Same
Nasafi argues that divine will is identical to creation of actions, with a logical consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the issue of divine will is not separate from the creation of actions, and why Maturidi theologians treat them together.

Core Idea
If God creates all actions, He wills them — no separate proof needed.

This lesson from al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla clarifies a key methodological principle in Maturidi theology. The discussion of God's will for human actions might seem to require its own proof, but al-Nasafi shows it follows directly from the established doctrine that God creates all actions. Yet because the early scholars (al-salaf) treated it as a primary topic, later theologians follow their approach. Let's see how he makes this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation.
Notice it's one long sentence with a logical structure.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that the matter of divine will is in reality the very same matter as the creation of actions.
This is a strong identity claim, not just similarity.
3
Follow the logical reasoning
If we prove Allah creates all actions and is free (mukhtar) and not compelled (mudtar), then He must be willing (murid) in creating them. So no separate proof is needed.
This is a logical deduction from khalq al-af'al.
4
Understand the historical context
Despite the logical identity, the Salaf discussed it as an independent topic, so later scholars follow their method.
This shows respect for the tradition while maintaining logical rigor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذه المسألة في الحقيقة عين مسألة خلق الأفعال فإنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن الله تعالى خالق أفعال العباد كفرًا كان أو معصية أو طاعة ، والله تعالى مختار في فعله غير مضطر كان مريدًا في تخليقه إياه فلا حاجة بنا بعد ذلك إلى إقامة دليل مبتدأ إلا أن السلف لما تكلموا في المسألة على طريق الأصالة أتبعناهم وتكلمنا فيها.
Then this matter in reality is the very same matter of the creation of actions; for if we prove by evidence that Allah is the creator of the actions of the servants, whether disbelief or disobedience or obedience, and Allah is free in His action, not compelled, then He is willing in His creating it. So we have no need after that to produce a new proof, except that the predecessors (al-salaf) spoke about the matter in the way of being original, so we followed them and spoke about it.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage makes a methodological point: the debate about God's will for evil actions is not a separate issue but follows from the established doctrine of the creation of actions. The author (al-Nasafi) argues that if we have already proven that God creates all actions of human beings, including disbelief, obedience, and disobedience, and that God is free (mukhtar) and not compelled (mudtar) in His action, then it follows necessarily that God is willing (murid) in His creation of those actions. There is therefore no need for an additional standalone argument for divine will. However, because the earlier scholars (al-salaf) discussed this matter as a primary topic (ala tariq al-asala), the later scholars follow them in doing so. This explanation shows that the discussion of will is derivative, not independent, and that the real foundation is the doctrine of divine creation of actions (khalq al-af'al).

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-af'al)
مريد (murid)
مختار (mukhtar)
مضطر (mudtar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the issue of divine will is separate from the issue of creation, requiring its own proof.
Correction: The author shows they are the same: if God creates actions, He necessarily wills them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there no need for a separate proof for divine will after establishing the creation of actions?
A) Because the Salaf already proved it
B) Because if God creates actions, He necessarily wills them
C) Because the issue is unimportant
D) Because the Mu'tazila agree

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position is that God's will for actions is not a separate issue but follows from His creation of all actions and His freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi says that once you accept that God creates all your actions, you don't need a separate proof that He wills them. But do you truly live as if every action of yours—even your moments of doubt and rebellion—is directly willed by God? If you really believed that, how would it change the way you grieve your failures and celebrate your successes? What would you need to let go of to embrace that fully?
?
The early scholars (al-salaf) treated divine will as a separate topic even though it was logically reducible to creation. What in your own life do you treat as a separate 'issue' when it is actually the same as something deeper you are avoiding? Are you using intellectual distinctions to keep from facing the root of a spiritual problem?
?
If God creates all your actions, including your obedience and disobedience, does that make Him the author of evil? Or does His freedom and wisdom absolve Him? How does your answer affect your sense of personal responsibility? Will you use this truth to deepen your reliance on God or to excuse your negligence?

Six Scriptural Objections of the Mu'tazila on Divine Will
Al-Nasafi lists six scriptural objections of the Mu'tazila against Ash'ari divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six scriptural objections the Mu'tazila raise against the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah wills all things, including evil.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila use Qur'anic verses to argue that Allah does not will disbelief or sin.

Al-Nasafi presents the scriptural doubts (shubah sam'iyya) that the Mu'tazila employ to challenge the Ash'ari position on divine will. Each objection cites a specific verse to claim that Allah's will is limited to good and obedience. This lesson surveys these six objections so you can see the scriptural basis of the Mu'tazila argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice that the objections are introduced as 'shubah sam'iyya'.
2
Identify each objection and its verse
Find each objection: 51:56, 2:185, 6:148 and its continuation, 16:35, and 40:31. Note how each is used.
The polytheists' objection uses multiple verses.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila logic
For each verse, consider how the Mu'tazila interpret it to deny Allah's will for evil. Ask: What does the verse say, and why do they think it contradicts Ash'ari doctrine?
The Ash'ari response is that Allah wills all but loves only good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه المعتزلة فنقول: إن المعتزلة يتعلقون بشبه بعضها سمعية وبعضها عقلية. فأما السمعية فنحو قوله تعالى : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الحنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) أخبر الله تعالى أنه خلقهم جميعًا للعبادة، وأنتم تقولون إنه خلق الكفرة منهم والعصاة لا للعبادة بل للكفر والمعصية وقوله تعالى : يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ ) والكفر أعسر العسر، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا ﴾. فالله تعالى أخبر أنهم يقولون : لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ثم كذبهم في ذلك ورد قولهم بقوله : كَذَلِكَ كَذَّبَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ وقوله : كَذَلِكَ فَعَلَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ وأنتم تساعدون في المقالة من كذبه الله تعالى بنص كتابه وهو باطل، وقوله: وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) وعندكم يريد كل ظلم كان ويكون، وهو خلاف ما في الكتاب، وإكذاب الله تعالى في ما أخبر.
We say: The Mu'tazila cling to doubts, some of which are scriptural and some rational. As for the scriptural, for example His saying, exalted is He: 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' Allah, exalted is He, informed that He created all of them for worship, but you say that He created the disbelievers and the disobedient among them not for worship but for disbelief and disobedience. And His saying, exalted is He: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' – and disbelief is the most severe hardship. And His saying, exalted is He: 'Those who associate partners will say, "If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners."' So Allah, exalted is He, informed that they say: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' Then He belied them in that and rejected their saying by His saying: 'Thus did those before them belie' and His saying: 'Thus did those before them do.' And you support in the statement one whom Allah has belied by the text of His Book, and that is false. And His saying: 'And Allah does not intend wrongdoing for the servants' – and according to you He intends every wrongdoing that has been and will be, and that is contrary to what is in the Book, and an accusation of lying against Allah, exalted is He, in what He informed.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural objections of the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position that God wills all things including evil. Al-Nasafi enumerates six scriptural proofs (shubah sam'iyya) that the Mu'tazila use to argue that God does not will disbelief and sin. The first verse 'I have not created jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (51:56) is used to claim that God's purpose in creation is only worship, so He cannot will disbelief. The second verse 'Allah intends ease for you, not hardship' (2:185) contrasts with the claim that God wills disbelief which is the greatest hardship. The third and fourth verses (6:148, 6:148, and 16:35) report the polytheists' argument that if God had willed they would not have associated partners, but God rejects their saying, so the implication is that God does not will their shirk; but the Ash'ari would agree that God does not approve it but still wills it? The Mu'tazila argue that if God's will is effective, then He must will it. The fifth verse 'And Allah does not intend wrongdoing for the servants' (40:31) is used to deny that God wills injustice, but the Mu'tazila argue that according to the Ash'ari, God intends all wrongdoing. Thus they accuse the Ash'ari of contradicting the Qur'an.

Key Terms
شبه
سمعية
إرادة
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think these verses prove that Allah cannot will evil and that their interpretation is the only valid one.
Correction: The Ash'ari respond that Allah's will includes all things, but He does not love or approve evil. The verses cited by the Mu'tazila are about divine approval and command, not absolute will.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument from the verse 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' according to the Mu'tazila?
A) God created all for worship, so He cannot will disbelief.
B) God created only believers for worship.
C) The verse proves free will.
D) The verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use specific Qur'anic verses to argue that Allah does not will disbelief or sin, but the Ash'ari maintain that Allah's will encompasses all things while His approval is only for good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the excuse 'if Allah had willed, it wouldn't have happened' to avoid taking responsibility for your own sin? How does this lesson warn you against hiding behind divine will? Commit to one area of your life where you will stop blaming fate and start repenting today.
?
When you read the verse 'Allah intends ease for you', do you truly believe that includes the hardship of breaking a bad habit or facing a difficult truth? What would change in your daily actions if you saw every hardship as something Allah intends for your ultimate ease? Resolve to face one discomfort this week as an act of trust in His will.
?
The Mu'tazila used scripture to argue that Allah cannot will evil. But in your own heart, do you sometimes resent God for allowing evil or suffering? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to a will that is higher than your understanding? Write down one area of pain you will surrender fully to His will, acknowledging that He knows what you do not.
