Ash'ari and Maturidi Solutions
Nasafi presents the Ash'ari and Maturidi responses to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools reconcile God's disapproval of disbelief with its actual occurrence by divine will.

Core Idea
Two schools offer distinct explanations: Ash'ari restricts the verse to believers; Maturidi explains approval as deeming good, which would negate punishment.

After the initial rebuttal, Nasafi presents two theological positions that directly answer the objection. The Ash'ari school interprets the Quranic verse as referring only to believers, while the Maturidi school distinguishes between divine pleasure (deeming good) and divine will (intending what occurs). Both preserve God's wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari position
Read the part starting with 'ثم نقول: إن الأشعري يقول'. Notice how Ash'ari says God approves of disbelief as ugly and interprets the verse specifically.
This is a scriptural interpretation approach.
2
Read the Maturidi position
Read from 'وعلى قول مشايخنا' to the end. Focus on how Maturidis define approval (riḍā) as deeming good (istiḥsān) and why that cannot apply to disbelief.
This is a conceptual/linguistic approach.
3
Compare the two solutions
Notice how each school resolves the apparent contradiction. Ask: Which relies on textual specification? Which relies on semantic distinction?
Both preserve God's transcendence and justice.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Will and Oppression
Refutes Mu'tazila's argument using internal critique and linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila's use of the verse 'Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' relies on a critique of their concept of will and a linguistic analysis of the phrase.

Core Idea
The verse does not prove God cannot will others' oppression—only that He does not oppress.

The Mu'tazila often cite the Qur'anic verse 'And Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' to argue that God cannot will evil acts by creatures. In this passage, the author responds by first pointing out an internal inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's own doctrine of divine will, then providing a linguistic argument to show the verse's meaning is narrower. Let's examine this refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
Recognize that the Mu'tazila use the verse to claim God cannot will oppression, and note the author's first move: he invokes the Baghdad school's own view that God is not described with real will, so the verse would mean something else on their terms.
The Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila deny that will is a real attribute of God.
2
Follow the linguistic analysis
The author cites linguists who explain that the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression' can have two meanings: either 'I do not intend that you be oppressed (by anyone)' or 'I do not intend to oppress you.'
This distinction is key to narrowing the verse's implication.
3
See how the author specifies the correct meaning
Based on prior evidences, the author argues that the intended meaning is that God does not oppress anyone Himself, not that He never wills oppression done by others.
Thus, the verse does not contradict the possibility of God willing acts of oppression by creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) . قلنا على أصل البغدادين منكم لا يوصف الله تعالى بالإرادة حقيقة، وإذا ذكرت إرادته لفعله فمعناه أنه فعل فيكون تقدير الآية (٢) - على زعمهم – وما ظلم الله ظلمًا ونحن هكذا نقول فلم يكن لهم في الآية حجة. ثم نقول : إن أهل اللغة قالوا : إذا قال الرجل لآخر لا أريد ظلمك كان معناه لا أريد أن تظلم أنت من غير تعيين الفاعل، وإذا قال: لا أريد ظلمًا لك كان معناه لا أريد أن أظلمك. ونحن نقول : إن (۳) الله تعالى لا يريد أن يظلم أحدًا، على أن أكثر ما في الباب أن هذه اللفظة محتملة للمعنيين جميعًا إلا أنا نعين أحدهما وهو أن المراد منه لا أريد أن أظلمك لما سبق من الدلائل، والله الموفق.
And the answer to their attachment to His statement (and Allah does not intend oppression for the servants). We say: according to the principle of the Baghdadis among you, Allah Almighty is not described with will in reality, and if His will for His action is mentioned, its meaning is that He did it, so the estimation of the verse – according to their claim – is ‘And Allah did not oppress any oppression,’ and we say this, so there is no proof for them in the verse. Then we say: the linguists said: when a man says to another ‘I do not intend to oppress you,’ its meaning is ‘I do not intend that you be oppressed’ without specifying the doer, and when he says ‘I do not intend oppression for you,’ its meaning is ‘I do not intend to oppress you.’ And we say: Allah Almighty does not intend to oppress anyone, because the most that can be said is that this word is possible for both meanings, except that we specify one of them, which is that the intended meaning is ‘I do not intend to oppress you’ due to the preceding evidences, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument based on the Qur'anic verse "And Allah does not intend oppression for the servants" (Ghafir 40:31). The author first responds by noting that according to the Baghdad school of the Mu'tazila, God is not described with will in a real sense; rather, when they speak of His will for an action, they mean that He performs it. Thus, the verse, on their own interpretation, would mean 'He did not commit oppression.' The author agrees with this, so the verse does not support their position. Next, the author presents a linguistic argument: the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression' can mean either 'I do not intend that you be oppressed (by someone else)' or 'I do not intend to oppress you.' The author asserts that the correct meaning here is that God does not intend to oppress anyone Himself, based on other evidences. Therefore, the verse does not negate that God may will acts of oppression done by others, as He is just in His decree.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
ظلم (oppression)
بغداديون (Baghdadis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that God cannot will any evil acts, including those done by creatures.
Correction: The verse only negates that God Himself commits oppression, not that He wills oppression by others.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key point of the linguists' analysis of the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression'?
A) It always means the speaker will not oppress anyone.
B) It can mean either not intending the listener to be oppressed or not intending to oppress the listener.
C) It only refers to preventing oppression by others.
D) It is ambiguous and cannot be interpreted.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' does not prevent God from willing oppression caused by others; it only affirms His own justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's linguistic analysis challenge you to trust God's justice even when you don't understand His decree? What would it look like for you to submit to God's will without requiring Him to fit your standards of justice?
?
The author says the intended meaning is specified 'due to the preceding evidences.' Think of a time when you felt oppressed or witnessed injustice. What 'preceding evidences' in your faith or life can help you hold firmly to the belief that God Himself never oppresses, even when you cannot see the full picture? Are you willing to commit to that belief today, without demanding a full explanation?

The Mu'tazila Argument and the Ash'ari Response
Presents the Mu'tazila argument and the Ash'ari definition of foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila argue that willing foolishness makes one foolish, and how the Ash'ari school refutes this by distinguishing between the witnessed and the absent, and by defining foolishness as that which is prohibited.

Core Idea
Foolishness is defined by prohibition; God is not prohibited, so He cannot be foolish.

The Mu'tazila present a rational argument: whoever wills foolishness is themselves foolish, based on human experience. But this analogy fails when applied to God, because the rules of the witnessed world do not automatically apply to the absent (God). In this lesson, we examine the initial objection and the Ash'ari response, which shifts the definition of foolishness to what is prohibited, thereby excluding God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first few lines where the Mu'tazila present their claim: 'The one who wills foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed.'
Notice the use of 'shahid' (witnessed) and 'gha'ib' (absent).
2
Understand the initial response
The author replies by giving examples of what would be truly foolish for God, but clarifies that God does not will such things.
The example of Pharaoh shows that God's will is consistent with His knowledge.
3
Learn the Ash'ari definition
Al-Ash'ari defines foolishness as what is prohibited. Since there is no prohibition on God, He cannot be foolish.
This definition resolves the issue by focusing on the presence of prohibition.
4
Reflect on the shahid/gha'ib distinction
The key move is that analogy from witnessed to absent is invalid without a common basis. The Ash'ari response uses this distinction.
This is a common method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما شبهتهم المعقولة فقولهم : إن مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد. قلنا: إرادة ما لو كان لأوجب زوال ربوبيته، وإرادة أن لا يكون ما لو لم يكن لزالت ألوهيته ،سفه، وقد مر أنه لو أراد إيمان فرعون فكان ولم يرد كفره فلم يكن مع أنه علم أن كفره يكون وإيمانه لا يكون لكانت إرادة تجهيل نفسه وهو سفه. ثم نقول : السفه عند الأشعري وأهل الحديث ما نهى عنه، وفي الشاهد مريد السفه منهي عنه فكان به سفيهًا، وفي الغائب لا نهي فلم يكن سفيها، وعندنا السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: (وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا) سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة (بَلْ القُوا) وقال أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة وهو كفر، وإن قالوا : كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها. ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفيه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال على أن كثيرًا من أصحابنا لا يطلقون أنه أراد شتم
As for their rational argument, it is their saying: ‘The one who wills foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed.’ We say: Willing what, if it occurred, would necessitate the removal of His lordship, and willing that what, if it did not occur, would remove His divinity, is foolishness. And it has passed that if He willed the faith of Pharaoh and it occurred, and He did not will his disbelief and it did not occur, while He knew that his disbelief would occur and his faith would not occur, then that would be willing to make Himself ignorant, which is foolishness. Then we say: Foolishness according to al-Ash'ari and the people of hadith is whatever is prohibited. In the witnessed, the one who wills foolishness is prohibited from it, so he becomes foolish by it. In the absent, there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. According to us, foolishness is that which is not attached to a praiseworthy consequence. So if it is attached to the will of foolishness, it would not be foolishness, just as it was not foolishness from the son of Adam (peace be upon him) in his saying: ‘I intend that you bear my sin and your sin’ (Quran 5:29), even though it was a will of foolishness. And it was not foolishness from Moses (peace be upon him) when he said: ‘And harden their hearts so that they do not believe’ (Quran 10:88). So similarly from Allah Almighty. And likewise, Moses (peace be upon him) when he said to the magicians: ‘Rather, you throw’ and said: ‘Throw what you are going to throw’ – was that throwing foolishness or wisdom? If they say: it was, then they make the opponent of the messenger in the miracle by what the magician brings of deception to be wisdom, which is disbelief. And if they say: the throwing was foolishness, we say: Did Moses will the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: no, then they claim that he did not will the appearance of his proof and the invalidation of what they oppose his proof with, and they willed the state of his messengership to remain in the realm of ambiguity and hesitation. And if they say: yes, then they admit that he willed foolishness and was not made foolish by it because under his will was the appearance of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. In our case, under the will of foolishness is the realization of His knowledge and His report, so it becomes wisdom, not foolishness. And it is said to them: If a messenger informs his people that such-and-such a disbeliever will come to me, insult me, and intend to kill me, does he will the realization of that in order to show his truthfulness in his report about the unseen, making it a miracle for him, or does he will that it not occur so that his lie becomes apparent and his miracle is not realized? If they say: the first, then they admit that the one who wills foolishness is not foolish if it has a praiseworthy consequence. And if they say: the second, then their obstinacy and stubbornness appear to people. As for their statement that the one who wills foolishness for himself in the witnessed is foolish, its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of acts, and many of our companions do not apply that He willed insult
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila's rational argument that 'one who wills foolishness is foolish,' based on analogy from human experience. The author first presents the objection and then responds by defining the terms differently. The author notes that willing something that would negate divine lordship or divinity is indeed foolish, but that is not the case for God. He then discusses the example of Pharaoh: if God had willed Pharaoh's faith while knowing it would not happen, that would be self-deception and foolishness – but God does not will such contradictions. Next, the author distinguishes between definitions of foolishness: al-Ash'ari and Ahl al-Hadith define it as what is prohibited; since there is no prohibition on God, He cannot be foolish. The Maturidi definition (according to the author) is that foolishness is whatever does not have a praiseworthy outcome. Several Quranic examples are given: Adam's son's statement 'I intend that you bear my sin' (5:29) is a will of foolishness but not considered foolish because it aimed at a good consequence (avoiding further sin). Similarly, Moses' prayer to harden Pharaoh's heart (10:88) was not foolish because it was a request for justice and manifestation of power. The author then uses the story of Moses and the magicians: Moses told them to throw first, which seemed to allow showmanship, but it led to the demonstration of the true miracle. If this is foolishness, then it would be wisdom because it resulted in the proof of prophethood. The author further argues that if Moses willed that throwing, it was for a wise purpose, so he was not foolish. Similarly, God's will for evil deeds can have wise outcomes such as the realization of His knowledge and the fulfillment of His decree. Finally, a hypothetical scenario is presented: a prophet predicts a disbeliever's insult; if he wills it to happen to confirm his prophecy, that is wise, not foolish. This supports the point that the outcome determines foolishness, not the mere act of willing. The passage concludes by noting that many Maturidi scholars do not even apply the term 'will' to God's decree of evil, but rather speak of it as His creation.

Key Terms

إرادة
شاهد
غائب
نهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That willing evil necessarily makes one evil or foolish.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that foolishness is defined by prohibition, not by the act of willing itself. Since God is not subject to prohibition, He cannot be foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition in this passage, what is foolishness?
A) Anything that has a bad outcome.
B) Whatever is prohibited.
C) Whatever is willed by a human.
D) Anything that contradicts wisdom.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's analogy from human experience fails because God is not subject to prohibition; therefore, the Ash'ari definition of foolishness as prohibited acts exonerates God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself judging God's actions as if He were bound by the same rules as humans? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of comparing God to yourself? Ponder a specific time when you felt something God ordained was 'foolish'. How does the Ash'ari definition challenge that feeling?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that willing foolishness is always foolish, even for God. Are there any areas of your life where you assume God must think or act like you do? Write down one such assumption and then re-read the source to see how it refutes it. What can you do today to surrender that assumption?
?
If foolishness is defined by prohibition, then God, who is not prohibited, cannot be foolish. This means everything God wills is beyond the category of foolishness. Does your heart fully accept that? Or do you secretly believe some of God's decrees are senseless? Take a moment to confess any doubt and ask for certainty.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
7 min
The Maturidi Definition and Prophetic Examples
Presents the Maturidi definition and multiple prophetic examples to refute the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of foolishness as that which lacks a praiseworthy consequence, and how this is supported by examples from the prophets.

Core Idea
Foolishness is defined by the absence of a good outcome, not by the nature of the act willed.

Having refuted the Mu'tazila using the Ash'ari definition, the author now presents the Maturidi position: foolishness is that which has no praiseworthy consequence. This definition allows that even willing what seems foolish can be wise if it leads to a good outcome. The lesson explores prophetic examples, including the son of Adam and Moses, to illustrate this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi definition
Read the line: 'وعندنا السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة'. Note the emphasis on consequence.
This is the core of the Maturidi position.
2
Examine the example of Adam's son
The son of Adam said 'I intend that you bear my sin' which is a will of foolishness, but it was not considered foolish because it had a good intention (avoiding sin).
The act is judged by its outcome.
3
Examine the example of Moses
Moses prayed for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened and later told the magicians to throw first. Both seem foolish but had wise outcomes.
Moses' actions were part of a divine plan.
4
Understand the hypothetical scenario
A prophet predicts a disbeliever's insult; if he wills it to happen to confirm his prophecy, that is wise.
This reinforces the principle of praiseworthy consequence.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
The author concludes that God's will for evil deeds can be wise because it fulfills His knowledge and decree.
This answers the Mu'tazila objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a will for something harmful or foolish always makes the willer foolish.
Correction: The Maturidi definition shows that the outcome matters; if a praiseworthy consequence follows, the will is wise, not foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi definition in this passage, what makes an act not foolish?
A) It is willed by a prophet.
B) It leads to a praiseworthy consequence.
C) It is prohibited by law.
D) It is done in private.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school defines foolishness by the absence of a good outcome, allowing that even willing apparent evils can be wise if they serve a greater good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made that others considered foolish but you believed would lead to a good outcome. How did you justify it? Does this lesson's criterion of praiseworthy consequence validate your reasoning or expose a flaw?
?
When you reflect on your intentions, do you ever hide behind 'good outcomes' to excuse actions that are clearly harmful? What concrete step can you take today to align your actions with wisdom as defined by praiseworthy consequences?
?
The prophets willed what appeared foolish to achieve a greater good. In what area of your life are you unwilling to risk appearing foolish for the sake of a higher purpose? What is holding you back?
